“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNSY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tb.e Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government,
One Person, one Vote.
Comolete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its_ lines. The result of its powerful
influence .and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over’ whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend..
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners -in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
oil Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is, the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its,vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and moat
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

per Annum.

all remittances are payable. Editorial communi.
ations to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay hy Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered hy mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with ivhorn we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleoo, Managing Director
Literary Communications to he addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions, submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings, All
communications ivill be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified cend stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly Under-
stand that ive are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 24, 1894.
A Dying Fetich..
The Gladstone Cabinet is a thing of the
past in England, and almost the first public
appearance of its successor, the Rosebery
Cabinet, has been to meet with defeat at the
hands of the Democratic section of the Com-
mons. The Cabinet has tranquilly ignored this
vote of censure, and has gone upon the even
tenor of its way with lordly indifference ;
yet, nevertheless, its prestige is woefully
damaged. The vote, it is true, was passed
in an ordinary House —that is to say, in one
that was barely half-full, and the Govern-
ment was defeated by a majority of only
two ; moreover, it seems to be generally
acknowledged that in a full gathering of the
Commons it has a tolerably solid majority.
But otherwise the event is eminently
significant. The man who defeated the new
Ministry was Labouchere the Radical
politician whom the House has so long
refused to regard seriously, and who was
despised and rejected when the last Glad-
stone Government was in process of forma-
tion ; the 147 members who supported him
represented the strongest truly democratic
vote yet recorded in the British Commons ;
and the resolution which they carried
against all the available voting - capacity
of the Rosebery Cabinet was one
demanding that the House of Lords should
be deprived of its power to veto measures
passed by the Commons—in effect, that the
Lords, as a legislative body, should be prac-
tically abolished. And the fact that a branch
of the British legislature should carry such a
resolution in any house, however thin, and
by any majority, however small, is both
astonishing and gratifying. It is, without
exception, the most hopeful British Parlia-
mentary event of the century. No matter
how slow its progress the world keeps
moving, and as it moves even the pro-
foundly reverential Briton loses some
measure of his reverence, and the divinity
which hedges in a lord gets worn away by
the gradual attrition of the ages.
No one who has not lived for many years
in England can understand how deeply-
rooted the British peerage is, and how much
more deeply rooted it, was a very few years
ago, in the soil of Britain. The country is
old, and it has a reverence for everything
which is ancient and pretentious and utterly
useless. It grovels to anything in the way
of dignified lumber which professes to have
its origin away back in far antiquity.
There is a halo about the British peerage
because of its presumable ancientness ;
the moss of grey tradition has grown over
it. It is a memory of the buried ages
when the early British dignitary went forth
in armour and rattled like a crate of loose
hardware down the paths of history, and it
rightly belongs to the period of monks and
tournaments and pursuivants and heralds. Its
existence is a ghastly attempt to jointheTenth
CenturyontotheNineteenth. Theold nobility
was created, in the first place, to be the mili-
tary force of the country ; the new nobility
is created because it is a coarse, thick-set
person who successfully floated a huge
brewery. The existing House of Beers is
reverenced because it is venerable and his-
toric ; yet, by the irony of circumstances, it
is, in the main, neither venerable nor his-
toric. It consists mostly of brewers and
coal-dealers and all manner of commonplace
traders ; and its real name is mostly Jones,
though it generally calls itself Percy or
the like ; and its pedigrees, like that of the
Duke of Fife, for example, are largely made
up of bogus family records, and stolen monu-
ments, and sham tombstones set up to the
memory of imaginary ancestors. It has
been held in esteem for many years because
its names were associated with many glorious
mediaeval butcheries, and the fact that about
two-thirds of it was plain, modern, commer-
cial Jenkins, which had only borrowed these
historic cognomens, made practically no
difference to a credulous nation reared in an
atmosphere of unquestioning veneration.
* # *
The British peers held their hereditary
rights in the first place because they formed
the hereditary fighting force of the State.
When they ceased, in a great measure,
to be personally a fighting force, they con-
tinued to hold their position because they
furnished, or were understood to furnish,
the army, and they held their estates solely
in consideration of so doing. When they
shifted their military responsibilities on to
the nation at large they retained their
power because they were an ancient and
more or less picturesque institution. And
when wars, wholesale executions, insurrec-
tions, proscriptions and other disasters prac-
tically extinguished the old nobility, and a
new nobility sprung up which had mostly
bought its titles for cash, or acquired them
because it was descended from a king’s mis-
tress, and which was not even picturesque or
historical, it held its place still because the
habitof reverence had eaten into the soul of the
nation and the desire for something to grovel
to had become a matter of second nature.
The peerage long ago deliberately abjured
all the responsibilities of its position, and
retained the privileges only. The modern
House of Lords is mostly as new, as garish,
and as utterly devoid of all the graces —if
there be any—of antiquity as the nigger
peerage which the Emperor Dessalines
created in Hayti. It is not better educated
—in fact, it is much worse educated—than
the Commons ; it is not more moral; it is
vastly less patriotic ; it is so utterly neglect-
ful of its legislative duties that out of some
560 peers it is seldom possible to get more
than 40 together in the gorgeous chamber
where they are supposed to mismanage the
affairs of the nation ; and it hardly contains
three men of ability who have been born in
the purple. It does nothing in return for
its right to overrule the deliberate resolution
of thirty-nine millions of people, save that it
exists, and even that it does badly and with
much indecency.
* # #
The history of the House of Lords for the
present century can be related in a few
words. It fought desperately against
Catholic emancipation in Ireland ; against
the admission of Jews to Parliament; against
each successive extension of the franchise,
beginning with that original Reform Bill
which first created anything which could
reasonably be called a franchise at all. It
opposed to the uttermost the extinction of
negro slavery, the wiping out of the tyran-
nous Irish Church, the ballot, the restriction
of the hanging laws, the abolition of the
brutal statutes which made trades-unionism
a crime to be expiated only by transporta-
tion under all the horrors of the Convict
System, and the partial mitigation of the
iniquitous Game Laws whereby the starving
peasant who killed a hare was sent out to
herd with the worst of criminals in Yan-
demonia or Norfolk Island. And it struggled
desperately against compulsory education,
against Home Rule for Ireland, against the
mitigation of the Irish land laws, against the
local Government system—against every-
thing, in fact, which bore the slightest resem-
blance to progress or enlightenment. These
are a few items in its history for the present
century, and its history in past centuries has
been the same. If it had had its way
Britain would have been a slave-holding
empire to this day. There would be no
franchise worth speaking of in Britain, and,
as an almost inevitable consequence, none
in the other parts of the empire, and
Australia would be still a Crown colony
bossed, probably, by the infamous Rum-
selling Corps, or some worthy successor of that
unspeakable body. There would be no free
press, no educational system, no local govern-
ment —in fact, the British empire would still
be very much as it was two centuries ago,
and, therefore, very much as the Russian
empire is to-day.
• • •
In all the records of the House of Peers
for at least two hundred years, there cannot
be found one concession to liberalism granted
except under extreme pressure. It has up-
held every old-time barbarity to the last,
and has struggled to the utmost against every
new idea. It has opposed everything, and
it has originated absolutely nothing. Yet
the British nation, until a very few years
ago, clung to it with the abject reverence of
a Fantee to his god. The beginning of the
new revolt has its origin in the passing of
the first Education Act—the greatest legis-
lative idea of the century—some 23 years
ago. Previous to that event the ignorance
of England, Ireland and Wales was blank,
abject, pitiable, and almost beyond the com-
prehension of the present generation, and
even that of Scotland, though by far the
least illiterate section of the kingdom, was
sufficiently vast. It was on this igno-
rance that the ancient incubus lived, and
when that national obscurity began to
be dispelled, the end was at hand. The
resolution which was passed the other day
in the Commons was carried by the vote of
the new generation—the one which has
grown up under the system of secular
education, and has lost its awe of the old-
time fetiches in the process. And the reason
why that vote was not absolutely overwhelm-
ing was that the old generation of the pre-
education period still forma most of the
voting power of Great Britain. When it has
died and been gathered to its illiterate
fathers the new era will begin in real earnest,
and the House of Eldest Sons will presently
be only the memory of a bygone curse, and a
nightmare that has passed away.
Dictator versus Prevaricator.
Dibbs is bad; but then Parkes is a thousand
times worse. The moral and political idiosyn-
cracies of the two men are configured in their
physical characteristics ; in both the Dictator
has the advantage over the Prevaricator.
Parkes prides himself on being thought “ a
nice, clean old man ” ; and many people, by
merely regarding him from a distance, are con-
vinced that he is all he looks. But examine
him closely—physically, socially, politically—
and he will appear decrepit, crooked and ugly,
unless you have a moral squint. Dibbs,
physically, at any rate, is stalwart, straight and
handsome. Morally and socially he is reput-
ably clean and apparently decent; commer-
cially and politically, he is downright honest
and courageous. Admitted that be is a blunderer
and a bully, there is no- sinuous sneakery or
greasy treachery in his composition ; and if he
is bumptious towards his equals and brutal to
hungry unemployed, lie proves himself to be just
as big a bully towards Imperial military-man
Hutton, who claims the right to play at
soldiers with Australian people’s money accord-
ing to his own English notions. The pompous
and cunning Parkes wouldn’t have done it so ;
he might have done it secretly and in a cowardly
fashion, but never as Dibbs did it. The be-
baubled Parkes, of Brummagem, was com-
pletely muzzled long ago. The bebaubled
Dibbs, of New South Wales, is too daring and
obstreperous to suffer the gag for long. All
bullies are tyrants, but tyrants are not always
cowards. Beside these negative personal quali-
ties, Dibbs has positively good ones. If he is a
public blunderer, he is privately above re-
proach. His best claim to esteem and confi-
dence rests on his personal probity and the scru-
pulous honour of his business transactions with
other men, especially in his dealings with
creditors. Of him it can truthfully be said that
he believes that “ honour does consist in paying
20s. in the £ ” —some people don’t. I wish not
to seem irreverent, but if “an honest man’s
the noblest work of God,” then ought He to be
praised for putting Dibbs in Parkes’ place,
because, despite that high-handed £IOOO episode,
Dibbs’ presence there brings us a step —if only
a short one—nearer to “pure and honest
Government,” which Parkes prates about so
persistently and ignores so consistently.
# * #
Bumptious tyrants and gruff dictators have
their uses, if only to suppress plausible
impostors and displace oily humbugs, and
thus prepare the way for better men. Under
the reign of Parkes and his successive
teams of insignificant and obsequious poli-
tical tpadies, the people were perpetually
deluded ; under the recreant Republican Dibbs
they are insulted and outraged. There’s more
hope for a people smarting under a sense of insult
and injury than for one bulldozed into the
belief that it is being righteously governed by
the best men when all the while it is being
misgoverned and exploited by a clique of “ the
crimps and blacklegs ” of public life. The
“Devil’s Advocate” could say little to the
public or private credit of the personally friend-
less Parkes, although in the columns of the
shoddy newspapers he is the most bepraised
and bepuffed man in Australia ; whereas much
can be said for Dibbs who, with all his faults,
has the one signal advantage of being (though
a man beloved of his personal acquaintances)
the most misrepresented and best-abused man
against whom the daily press ever “took a set.”
If he had never done anything else than oust
Parkes and keep h i in and his parasites out of ofti ce
he would have deserved well of his native land.
The political pettifogger Parkes claims the
palm of statesmanship, and many concede it to
him, although he has no more title to it than he
has to the “ Dunmow Flitch of Bacon.” Over
and over again it has been proved that he never
passed a bad law that was not his own, nor a
good one that was. His claim to original
statesmanship rests mainly on the Treason
Felony Bill, a proposal to build a big dead-house
in Sydney Centennial Park, another proposal to
steal the name of the whole continent to confer
it upon the province of New South Wales, and
■—his bankruptcy schedules. These are about
his only original acts attempted or done ; all
the others he claims as his are either plagiarised
from other people without acknowledgment or
copied from other statutes and botched in the
copying.
* * *
Now, what shall be said further for the
behoof of the bebaubled bully Dibbs ? To start
with, he is native-born ; and ere he entered
Parliament was the leader of the movement in
favour of “ free, compulsory, and secular ”
education, while Parkes was currying favour
with the churches and the classes as an agitator
for State - aided denominational education.
Yet Parkes claims and gets the credit of
being the author of the New South Wales
public school system ! Then Dibbs was the
man who stopped the wholesale alienation of
the public estate which Parkes and his gang,
crying, “ Prosperity ! Prosperity ! !” had been
selling by auction to the extent of £2,000,000
a year. It was his Government which first
proposed a land-tax in New South Wales, a
proposal which Parkes and his myrmidons
defeated ; and he belonged to the Government
which, to an extent, reformed the Land Act, in
1884. Whether regarded as the outcome ■ of
conviction or a concession to expediency, his
breaking with the old “shoddy ring” and
espousal of Protection was a step distinctly
Australian, and in that sense patriotic, though
it made him many rich and powerful enemies.
The conversion or perversion of Dibbs to Pro-
tection dealt a death-blow to the Preetracle
fetish and the long-established rule of one-
impostor-and-his-tail. It suited the press to
denounce Dibbs as the manufacturer of
Parkesian deficits, but by sheer grit and bull-
dog courage (and the forceful assistance of Toby
Barton) he fought it down.
* * #
And, after all, though Dibbs is a disgraced
Republican renegade and the wearer of a
Downing-street badge, he’s neither better nor
worse for that than a dozen other recreant
Australians ; and he has certain qualities which
still make him tolerable to tolerably honest
men, where Parkes and such are positively in-
tolerable. These cringe and crawl to their Im-
perial masters like the servile sycophants they
are; Dibbs hobnobs with royalty, his hat
askew, and puffing a big, fat cigar, with the
same insolent audacity that prompted him to
poke his stick into Queen Elizabeth’s bed to
see what it was stuffed with. He is blunt,
brazen and bumptious ; but he is neither false,
faithless nor fri volous. He changes his political
intentions as quickly as a weathercock veers,
but in the matter of personal promises his yea
is yea and his nay, nay, no matter whom "he
displeases. Despite the combined opposition
of three powerful parties, he has done more
solid legislative work within the last two years
than Parkes ever did in ten. Loaded with the
legacy of debt and arrears of work left him by
Parkes, he has managed to keep the province
going in spite of universal distress and general
depression. He may not be the “ saviour of his
country,” but it is difficult to deny him some
credit for his prompt and courageous action
during the late financial crash. People differ
as to the merit and ultimate effect of that
action, but everybody sees cause for congratula-
tion that Parkes was not in power at the time.
If Dibbs does curse and swear at Labour
politicians, the unemployed, and payment of
members, even that is better than bamboozling
people by soapy promises never intended to be
fulfilled. It is less odious to be kicked than to be
slavered. To Dibbs’ Government are due, at least
in part, the new electoral law, such as it is ; the
Coal Mines Regulation Act; the establishment
of experimental village-settlements, as well as
a share of the credit for retrenchment, besides a
number of other progressive measures. In the
present chaos of New South Wales parties and
confusion of opinions, Dibbs is a better man
than any of the numerous competitors for his
position.
THE DEVIL’S ADVOCATE.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Policeman and His Gun.
The recent shooting episode at Broken Hill is
another pleasant commentary on New South
Wales Premier Bibbs’ foolishness in arming the
police with small cannon and turning them
out to plug the community promiscuously. So
far, only the police-version of the story is
available, and The Bulletin accepts it with-
out question on the ground that it is sufficiently
ugly for all purposes. There were two police-
men in the case, and one common drunk. In
the course of an altercation the drunk hit
one of the officers, whereupon the other
clubbed him on the head. Then the inebriated
one fetched a crowbar, with which he broke one
constable’s rib and furnished the other with a
wound on the scalp. Considering that the odds
in favour of the force were two to one, and that
a crowbar is a hideously cumbersome weapon
compared to a baton, it is difficult to under-
stand why the police did not stun their oppon-
ent just here, if he couldn’t be captured
otherwise, and run in. This is what
they would have done in the pre-re-
volver era, but now the “cop” has learned
to rely on his “gun” instead. So the
pair left their uncaptured prey in the
street and “ ran to the police-station ” for their
artillery. Apparently they didn’t ask anyone
to assist them, or try to get a reinforcement;
they simply went straight for their weapons.
Then they came back, and finding their
opponent zig-zagging homewards, they didn’t
seize him, but simply ordered him to stop. As
he didn’t stop the pair opened fire on their one
miserable drunk, and, after missing him with
several execrably bad shots at close range,
they plugged him fatally in the stomach. Then
one officer “closed with” the dying man
and “threw him down,” and he gave up the
ghost five minutes later. A few weeks ago
The Bulletin remarked that it was now evi-
dently regarded as a capital offence not to stop
when ordered to do so by a policeman, and its
words have come true. The spectacle of two


Evidence —Facts prepared to fit the occa-
sion.
Constable Howlett, after 14 years’ service
in the British army and an equal term in S.A.
police, recently so far forgot the buttered side of
his bread as to make serious charges against some
of his superior officers, whom he accused of appro-
priating much valuable public property to their
private use. He was at once suspended, and was
a week afterwards dismissed for alleged “drunken-
ness and insubordination,” although the previous
charge had never been preferred against him, and
the “ insubordination ” consisted in his outspoken-
ness. Now the authorities resolutely refuse
either to hold an enquiry into the case or to pay
him his retiring allowance (.£170). They appar-
ently fear lest his evidence should cause a scandal,
involving important personages. The Labour-
party should force on an enquiry. Howlett is
clearly entitled to a daylight trial.
policemen, whose tempers were probably in-
flamed by the crowbar episode, deliberately
going for their revolvers and with them bom-
barding to death an unarmed wretch at about
ten paces range, is sufficiently revolting. It
isn’t even decent lynch law, for the policemen,
by their own showing, were the injured parties,
the judges, jury and executioners all in one.
Also, the fact that the officers missed with five
shots at close range is pleasant reading for the
community at large, seeing that anybody is liable
to be in the road when an excited constable is
firing wildly into space. In the present instance,
one bullet came very near to killing a woman
w.ho was asleep in her own bed, but the com-
parative thinness of the population prevented
any general assassination being done. Such
an episode, in Sydney, would have almost
certainly led to the death of somebody.
The revolver in the hands of an excited Irish
constabulary is evidently going to make much
trouble. If Premier Dibbs insists on the con-
tinued use of that deadly weapon he had better
abolish the present Hibernian police-force and
start a Dutch one instead. The Dutch are a
stolid, slow-going, broad-beamed race built on
the lines of a Chinese junk, and are about the
only nation which thinks twice beforejit shoots
once, when it is clothed in a little brief authority
and a gun.
-♦
A grave mistake is being made by progressive
politicians in devoting all their attention to
public law and allowing private law to shift for
itself. The result is that while most states are
acquiring constitutions nominally freer than of
old, many of the old bonds bearing upon the
most ordinary relations of life still rankle.
Considering the numbers affected and the way
in which they are affected, the law of distress
is a worse evil than plural voting; the chicanery
of insolvency proceedings is more productive of
corruption among the people than a big railway
bill; the control by the dead over the living
by means of the doctrines of wills and adminis-
tration is more unnatural than the pos-
sibility of being ruled by the imbecile scion of
a successful pirate ; the lawyer system is a
worse cancer than even possible anarchy, the
joint stock co. system affects more people and
has more pitfalls, quagmires and class privi-
leges and perquisites than the whole question
of Royalty and Republicanism can supply ; and
the subject of marriage affords more scope for
reform and would have a more potent effect on
the happiness of the people than Australian and
Imperial Federation together. Neglect in mat-
ters of private law brings its revenge. It
tends to introduce irresponsible and unrepre-
sentative legislators. It has always happened
that when the constituted lawmakers have
neglected private laws, the Judges have made
them. And they are doing so in Australia
to-day.
New South Wales Labour-members should
fight hard for Cook’s “Truck Bill.” At pre-
sent some mine-owners “expect” their em-
ployes to patronise a certain store, or pub.,
which store or pub. is the mine-owners’ own
property. The miner who doesn’t observe this
“ expectation ” is usually found incompetent or
undesirable on some early pretext, and—joins
the unemployed. The store commodities are at
prices highly overtopping current retail rates.
The same villainies are practised in the camps
of public works contractors, in which cases the
scheme generally takes the form of a friend of
the contractor running a shanty adjacent to the
camp, or even of an enterprising stranger pay-
ing a percentage fee to the contractor for the
privilege of opening such shanty. These dens
call themselves “ boarding-houses,” but the
teetotal navvy, or other worker, has a short
stay on the contract. Cook’s Bill would abolish
all such coercion, and would compel employers
to pay full wages in hard cash without any
deductions for “ truck.” The next step should
be to abolish the whole system of “ garnishee
orders,” as an evil blight on the industrial com-
munity.
The Australian Star puts in a plea for the
Afghan camel-driver and the Mongolian :
The Sovereign of Great Britain and its dependencies has
consented to permit the people of one dependency to
assume the ri”ht to restrict the liberty of fellow subjects
of the same Sovereign to come among them. Similarly
the Imperial authorities of the great Chinese Empire have
but faintly objected to their subjects being treated as
semi-enemies by the subjects of a friendly Power. These
are conditions wfiich cannot be expected to endure for
ever. Moreover, inasmuch as the objections to the im-
migration and presence of the aliens in question are not
racial or moral, but distinctly economic, it matters very
little whether the Chinaman, the Afghan or Syrian be
excluded altogether, their own Governments continuing
too weak or too indifferent to insist on their being treated
with benevolence. Every race or nationality which has
been depressed into habits of labouring for trifling wage,
and of living on the cheapest scale, presents just as
objectionable features as the swarthy Afghan, the dark
Hindoo, the yellow Mongolian. The Sicilian and Italian
peasant, and the Levantine Greek, are just such people,
and they have already begun to discover Australia.
This is very good if regarded as eloquence, but
considered as information it is a failure. The
Sovereign of Great Britain isn’t the person who
has “consented ” to allow Australia to restrict
Chinese immigration ; the “ consent ” of that
crowned shadow hasn’t been seriously asked in
connection with any legislative act for many
years past. The Chinaman, who is the person
restricted, isn’t, except to an infinitesimal
degree, a “fellow-subject.” The “Imperial
Chinese authorities ” can’t object with any show
of reason to the Australian policy of restriction,
seeing that the Australian or other vyhite
man in China is fifty times more restricted.
“ British subjects ” have never had free access
to China—they have always been confined to
a few cities at or near the coast —and why the
Chinaman should demand for himself what he
doesn’t give to others passes comprehension.
Also, the argument about the Sicilian and the
Italian and the Portuguese and the miscel-
laneous Levantine is abject folly. All these
admit the “ British subject ” freely to their own
countries, and give him about as much legal
protection as he can get in London, so
that their free admission in return is
at least something resembling a fair ex-
change. And—what is vastly more im-
portant—they are not aliens in the Chinese and
Afghan sense. In reasonable numbers they can
be absorbed in the general mass of the popu-
lation without any injury to the national char-
acter, and they are objectionable only because
they tend to reduce the scale of wages. If they
didn’t affect the labour-market there would be
no more objection to them than to Premier
Rosebery or any other European. But the
Asiatic can’t be absorbed, and even if his
presence raised the wage-rate until it was as
high as Mount Koscuisko he would remain the
same old racial curse to the bitter end.
In Melbourne, as in Sydney, the lawyer who
“misconducts” himself in money matters is
expanding into a colossal joke. Last week
J udge Hood dealt with the case of an attorney
who had hung on to his client’s cash in the now
epidemic style. £35 of a client’s money had
passed into the coffers of his adviser, on the
understanding that it was to be retained on
account of certain legal proceedings then pend-
ing. The client wisely discontinued these
proceedings when the costs —dear old costs !
had arrived at £lB Is., whereupon he held out
his hand for the balance due to him, £l6 19s,
The attorney couldn’t cash up, was summoned,
ordered to pay, didn’t pay. The affidavit filed
in defence stated the utterly irrelevant fact
that the whole £35 collected by this amusing
attorney would have been “absorbed” if the
client had not stopped the aforesaid proceedings
when only £lB Is. was “absorbed.” He bad
expected to set off “ costs ” against the full
amount, but his hopes were blighted. His in-
ability to absorb the lot was seemingly the
extenuating circumstance. Anyway, Judge
Hood took a benign view of the case. The
attorney, “by his own statement,” said the
Judge, “had done that which a solicitor had
no right to do. He had applied client’s money
to his own use, and when asked for it was not
able to comply with the request. (!!) That would
be little or no offence in a layman, but it was an
offence in a professional man. So far, the
lawyer was to blame. The case was, however,
deprived of aggravating circumstances.”
Heaven and Earth ! Doesn’t Judge Hood con-
sider his own libel upon laymen an aggravated
circumstance ?
A simple-minded genius named Buckhurst,
forming part of a deputation to Premier
Patterson, suggested that “ 120 men should be
selected from the ranks of retired Civil servants
and professional men (Mr. B. himself is a retired
builder by profession) to act as permanent
juries in Melbourne.” This is a splendid sug-
gestion from the Fat Man point of view. Every
retired Civil servant would bring a calm, un-
biassed mind to bear upon the case of the low,
vulgar beast who “ shook ” a pair of boots, and
when the erring bank-clerk, or polite Govern-
ment employe came along for trial it would be
bad luck for him if he didn’t have at least ten
mercifully-disposed people on the jury. The
Law hasn’t many terrors for Fat Man under
present conditions, but with a permanent jury
system —our oath ! He’d form a limited com-
pany for working the noble 120, the price of
verdicts to be fixed by a financial committee.
There would, of course, be a division of cases
into classes. The defendant’s payment in an
extra special first-cjass case would vary between
£250 for a simply/* 1 disagreement ” and £lOOO
for an acquittal without a stain on his
character.

Robert Reid, M.L.A., of Victoria, has evi-
dently got about the worst attack of swelled
head on record since he started on his muddled
embassy to Europe. This feeble bore now
mistakes himself and Patterson for Australia,
and speaks wildly of that continent as “ we.”
He was interviewed recently in London, and
this is the awful way he went on :
On the subject of Imperial defence I hold very strong
views. What would be the position of Australia, of all
our colonies, in fact, if the Imperial Government were to
allow the navy of Great Britain to sink below the strength
of the navies of the world? J will urge this upon Her
Majesty’s Government most strongly. We in Australia
are quite prepared to do our part—-to take our fair share
in the defence of the Empire. . . . I have a most im-
portant subject to discuss with the War Office, and that is
the supply of hydraulic-pressed chaff, bran and horse-feed
for the army. lam also in hopes of getting the English
'bus and tram companies to take up this matter. One
part of my mission is to bring under the notice of the
Imperial Government the necessity of a cable via the
Pacific, which shall pass only through the British
Dominions. The future of the new Canadian-Australian
steamship line will depend upon the success of my mis-
sion in opening up trade on a reciprocal basis with
Canada. . . . Although the external debt of Austra-
lasia may reach £188,000,000, there is a large balance to
credit with the ever-increasing output of wool and wine,
of meat and wheat, of silver and copper, of every kind of
product, in fact, and every penny that has been borrowed
is certain to be repaid, with interest. We have borrowed
freely. Admitted. But you have reaped the advantage.
We have not had the coin.
MTIWRAITH has now a special and cogent
reason for urging his old theory that Britain
should help to pay the expenses of emigrants to
Queensland. The land-grab-railway syndicators
will, when they come into daylight, stipulate, as
some of them did before, for the importation of
their own workmen. The current anti-alien
feeling will render it impossible to introduce
coolies or Chinese without a civil war, and thus
the syndicators will have to use up Australian
labour at Australian wages, or fall back on
England. MTlwraith is preparing for that
event. He considers that he can induce
Queensland to carry on assisted-iminigration,
and if he can also get Britain to “assist the
practical result will be the establishment of free
immigration, and the dumping down in the
North of shipload after shipload of the poor and
outcast. Half of MTlwraith’s official resig-
nations, half of his ordinarily-inexplicable
doings and sayings, have their origin in his
determination to rail Queensland on the land-
grant syndicate system, and this latest cabled
utterance of his, in face of the awful workless-
ness and destitution existing in Australia, is no
exception. The syndicators demand a con-
gested, and, therefore, a cheap, labour-market,
or the right to introduce Mongol and coolie
labour; MTlwraith knows that the latter
alternative means fire and dynamite ; therefore,
he will, if he can, make arrangements for the
former. Let the Democratic party sharpen its
teeth. Unless it bites the carotid artery of the
MTlwraithian party its fat will be used to
grease the syndicate’s railway-axles and its
bones to manure the canefields of Miss Shaw’s
Northern aristocracy.
Melbourne begins to realise that the people
who have ruled it and exploited it for so many
years, “ worked the little oracle on the religious
lay.” Museums and picture galleries couldn’t
be opened on the Saw bath, Sunday trains and
Sunday newspapers were to be vetoed, not on
any high moral ground, but merely to supply
the crowd with a continual object-lesson on
the saintliness of their rulers. But the boom-
burst has destroyed nearly every sanctified
reputation, and the poor, deluded citizens are
kicking themselves with savage energy when
they contemplate the mortification of the flesh
they have endured merely to supply a clique of
hypocritical scoundrels with a cheap means of
self-advt. The Bulletin has no hesitation
in writing that the men with the cleanest
public reputation to-day in Melbourne are
those whose names have never been mentioned
in connection with any church or Young
Men’s Christian Association, and who have
been regarded by the clergy and the ruling gang
of cold-water saints as dissipated and unclean.
Melbourne is now tired of placing “ char-
acter ” before capacity as a qualification for
prominence in public matters, and therein lies
a germ of hope for the future.
Apropos to N.S.W. Chief Justice Barley’s
zeal for the protection of public decency, is not
the too frequent occurrence of the names of
Barley, Owen, Foster, Manning, Stephen,
Innes, and Windeyer among the Judge’s Asso-
ciates something in the nature of an outrage on
public decency ? These Associates are almost
invariably sous or relatives of the Judges,
they receive £3OO a year and outrageous
pickings —we speak of the charges made for
copies of Judges’ notes —for exceedingly light
work, and they are all the time qualifying for
the profession of barrister, and scraping up an
extensive acquaintance with attorneys and their
articled clerks. There is an amount of cold-
blooded selfishness about the arrangement that
almost disarms criticism. Unfortunately, no
remedy seems available except an appeal to
their Honor’s sense of decency. And what is
true of N.S. Wales in this respect is unfor-
tunately also true of the rest of Australia.
The Bulletin directs the attention of Queens-
land Attorney-General Byrnes to what is
apparently a scandalous miscarriage of justice,
occurring at Toowoomba Police-court, on
March 7. A man was charged with a brutal
assault upon a little girl three years of age.
The mother of the woman and another married
woman gave evidence which would be grotesque
were it not horrible, proving the consummation
of the outrage. The child was seen coming out
of defendant’s house, and she immediately
made a complaint to her mother. Finally, the
child has been ill ever since. The defendant is
“ quite a respectable person,” and the greatest
exertions were made to quash the case.
Medical evidence was not forthcoming,
and the mother was asked to settle the
matter. The only defence set up. was that the
evidence was inadmissible, and the police-
magistrate had not the moral courage, in the
face of defendant’s lawyer, to send the case to
a jury, although he is reported to have said
that “ there could be no moral doubt as to the
commission of the offence.” The deduction to
be drawn is that, unless children are old enough
to be heard as witnesses, they may be out-
raged with impunity, on the ground that other
evidence is inadmissible. The Bulletin
refuses to believe that this is the law of Queens-
land, and in the interests of common humanity
calls upon the Attorney-General to bring the
“ respectable person ” to trial if the facts are
as reported in the local press, which there is no
reason to doubt.

Melbourne Age has the cruel effrontery to
declare that £600,000 a year has been saved to
Victoria “through the change of administration
in the Railway Bepartment.” Speight, who
was hurled out of office just as the depopulation
of Melbourne commenced, would not, according
to the Age suggestion, have dreamed of reduc-
ing expenses. The deficit would have included
another £600,000 up to date but for the vir-
tuous conduct of Baviu Syme in commanding
his trembling Shiels to get rid of Speight. This
ingenuous theory is based on the fiction that
Speight in 1891-’92 said “ nothing could be
saved”in 1893-’94, although a dozen banks might
break, and 100,000 people might go adrift in
the meantime. Speight, of course, said noth-
ing of the sort. He spoke of railway conditions
as they were before the smash came, and merely
rejected the economy proposals of the immortal
Wheeler because they were preposterous on
the face of them. Heaven (and perhaps Zox)
alone can say what Speight might have done in
a position which he didn’t occupy. What the
present Railway Commissioners might have
done instead of Speight & Co., had they been
the other people a few years ago, is as uncer-
tain as the possible whereabouts of Lot’s wife,
supposing she had been Noah’s mother.
Thus a correspondent:—
Isn’t it about lime that the periodic wooing of the coy
Governor should cease? Towards the close of every
Governor’s term there are the same heart-llutterings and
anxieties. “He will stay with us a little longer.” “No,
he won’t, ‘private affairs’ call him home.” "Yes, he
will.” “No, he won’t.” And he doesn’t, as a rule, hut
gets out of the place as soon as he decently can—or
sooner. Why should we go through the same old farce
of receiving cablegrams that So-and-so is going to reign
over us when the present figurehead retires ; then, that
So-and-so is not coming, that he wouldn’t think of it,
that Somebody-else may, that Another-one “has been
approached,” i.e., has been begged and prayed to come out
to these wilds for a time ? Then, when the Chosen One
does arrive, the joy and gladness shown by a grateful
public, though why we should be joyful goodness knows,
when we are all conscious that both His and Her Ex. will
become tired of giving the limp handshake and the well-
bred scowl, and will want to “chuck ” the whole thing in
a year. We can never get a satisfactory Gov. It stands
to reason that no man of ability could afford to leave the
stage of active affairs in London for so long a time as five
years. He would be forgotten and his chance of promo-
tion gone. We cannot get an able man, and we don’t
want the other sort. Now that Governor Hopb-
'ioun is going, Victoria might inaugurate a new
regime, and make the Chief Justice perpetual acting-
Governor with no extra pay. The other provinces could
follow suit, and then all the vice-regal salaries could be
saved, and our self-respect likewise. We shouldn’t have
to make door-mats of ourselves every five years for the
Colonial Office people to wipe their feet on. No doubt
such a proceeding as putting up “ To Let” boards on all
Govt. Houses would be but, then,
every new departure is “unconstitutional” until the
People make it constitutional, by taking it and keeping
on in that direction for ever after.

March 21, 1894.
My Dear Moobabinda—
Ada Crossley’s benefit at Sydney Town Hall
(which showmen have given up billing as the
“Centennial”) was a hujje pecuniary success.
Regarded as a spectacle, the over-crowded hall
suggested an excited ant-hill, with each ant dyed
a different colour. In the semi-circle of patronage.
Mayoress Manning sported a good-looking gown
of black armure, the sleeves outlined in steel
passementerie, the corsage trimmed in steel fringes
and black folds, diamond ornaments. Miss Felicia
Manning appeared in a Watteau dress of white
bengaline; Mrs. Burdekin, in cream and helio-
trope. Miss Burdekin’s silk gown was the colour
of a brickfielder with the sun behind it, and her
opera-cloak looked like a military saddle-cloth.
Miss Crossley wore white, of course, with thick
semi-bretelles of white feathers —a novelty that
would make a scraggy woman look like a French
hen.
On her first appearance, Ada Cross-ley—who
looks like Myra Kemble at a hundred paces—got
a loud reception which visibly affected her. I
thought her selection of songs un W’ise. After hearing
her warble Patey’s old stock success, “ Che faro,”
in an ernineptly thoughtful and pleasing manner,
but without the forty-horse-power effects that
Patey put into it, the idea occurred to me that
Europe may possibly discover a mezzo.-soprano in
our Ada, There are—so far—no organ-notes in
Miss C.’s voice-box—no rumbling sounds rever-
berate along the floor and up the legs of yoffr
chair as Patey’s subterranean chortlings used to
do, and if Miss Crossley is a contralto she will
probably be a low one judging from her sweet, but
forceless, low notes. Miss Crossley was assisted
by Lilian Tree, Henri Staell, Kowalski, Charles
Saunders (obliged to encore his encore “Take a
Pair of Sparkling Eyes ”), Frank Adams, Halle-
well, besides a strange, husky baritone, who made
his first and, I hope, last appearance, and the
Liedertafels, with Charles Huenerbein, Mr. Sykes,
and Signor Hazon, as accompanists.
If Miss Crossley comes back a prima-donna she
ceuldn’t evoke more enthusiasm than her benefit
did. All the florists’ shops in the town were
emptied at her feet; Sydney Liedertafel sent a
big floral lyre whizzing down a wire ladder like a
coal-shoot, and Mayor Manning neatly presented
her with an address for which she returned tearful
thanks from “ the bottom of her heart.” A satis-
fled thrill went thro’ the audience, as though they
had got their money’s worth, when Ada wiped her
syes with her fingers. Why did she cry? Well,
perhaps it was the Town Hall English.
Apropos. A sight of a recent concert was the
vision of a rival contralto who isn’t going Home
to develope the deep, deep G, enthusiastically
sending up a spiky bunch of pinks to the
soprano of the evening. Her little posy would
anly have been a drop in the bucket with the
heroine of the programme, but—set a singer to
snub a singer.
With the Crossley benefit still fresh in people’s
remembrance I would like to remind the generous
public of Sydney that there are one or two old
favourites in our midst who richly deserve a similar
compliment. Everybody remembers—for one—
Mr. Andrew Fairfax, the robust basso who, for so
many years, sang in company with the best
Artistes, but who never refused to give his valuable
services to any charity concert, no matter how
cbscure the object of it was. He is an old man
now, and I was grieved to hear the other day that
he is very poor and, through terrible ill-health,
unable to take employment. Mt would only be a
fair return to organise an attractive benefit for
this kindly singer who now so much needs what
he has so often cheerfully rendered to others.
I have received a copy of the “ Blue Mountains
Waltz,” by a lady composer—a squeaky tune in
ihree-four time which will not leave a bubble on
the sea of music, despite its dedication to the
latest Sydney ladyship. Which reminds me that
Maud Fitzstubbs’ “ Heather ” waltz has reached
its 13th edition—an unparalleled proceeding in
Australia—and is now the rage of Perth. Wes-
tralian showmen draw attention on their pro-
grammes to the fact that the orchestra will play
“the famous Heather Waltz, the rage of the
Eastern provinces.”
N.S.W. is this year to have a sort of abridged
prospectus of the Easter Encampment. Not the
full glory of a grand‘fit-out with all the officers
under canvas running short of afternoon-tea, and
inducing the reluctant society-girl visitor to take
split sodas instead, as used to happen in the good
old days—but a more sharp and business-like pro-
ceeding, I venture to say.
Whatever sort of a turn-out they afford, I only-
hope Major-General Hutton won’t man the field
with warriors mounted on the military bicycle—a
brand-new horror added to N.S.W. warfare, and
introduced, no doubt, to save forage. I met a
hero on a “safety ’’ only the other day careering
down a hill with his feet stuck out. He was a
gaudy soldier with a good many primary colours
to the square inch, and a rainbow band round ids
hat—a recruiting-sergeant would have looked a
inopoke beside him. I thought him like a
King .Lowry parrot on an egg-whisk, and for any
other purpose than advertising the performing
fleas or something of that sort, he would be a blot
on my vision.
I hear—in an oblique sort of way—that one or
two N.S.W. politicians, interested in the “Shall
we bury or burn ?” question, are trying to provoke
Guv’ment to build an oven wherein to cook the
dead. I hope they get it, though there are really
some most unsentimental points about cremation.
For instance, widows and widowers will re-marry
much earlier when we make snuff out of our
corpses than they do now. When the late John
Smith is burnt, he is annihilated and absolutely
got rid of. Even when he is burnt and his ashes
inurned on the widow Smith’s mantelpiece he is a
thousand million times more departed than he
would be were he buried. A burnt mmis as dead
as ever he will be, but, somehow or other, a
merely-buried one isn’t.
Apropos of the cool thousands legally demanded
from the fatherly co-respondent in a certain N.S.
Wales divorce-case and legally ordered to be
settled on the guilty wife and her child ; after this
is there any woman stupid enough to agitate for
female suffrage? Rather let woman revel in this
position—made plain as print by the Te Klcot
case—that Eve is not yet legally responsible for
throwing her cap over the mill as is poor hunted
Adam. While such decisions obtain, woman
should hold fast to the fashionable women’s-rights
that are masculine wrongs for the most part.
There is cheerful dressing to relieve the piebald
experiences of “ Hans the Boatman.’’ Henrietta
Watson was built for gored skirts, and gets a tight
fit in pale terra-cotta, and anon in cedar-brown,
but there is nothing to attract a woman’s opera-
glass in either garment. The realistic Miss W.,
by the way, gets in a very natural touch when she
sits on the water-lilies pa has just paid two (stage)
dollars for, in order to make room for Hans on
the rustic seat. What wom,an wouldn’t squash a
conservatory-full of ’em so that her summer-resort
beau may sit gracefully in her pocket? Which
further reminds me that “ Hans the Boatman ” is
an ideal play to finish up the summer season with.
When Gladys shows a disposition to tickle the
jolly young waterman under the chin one thinks
of the stories good-looking coach-drivers in the
recuperating districts have to tell you every
winter.
Charles Arnold, in a peppermint-striped Gondo-
lier cap topping his slate-grey suit, reminded me
of Charles Ryley with no grease-paint round his
eyes. Any diffident; youth who doesn’t know how
to approach his Venus ought to turn Hans into an
object-lesson. Mrs. Arnold, as Jeffie, sported
Jersey locks and a Dolly Varden dress in the first
act, but the neat grey gown and, blue sun-bonnet
was her nattiest rig-out—even beside the sweet
honeymoon toilet in chrysophrase-green silk, with
white insertion ad lih.^ and a light-and-airy hat.
Alluding to sun-bonnets, I am told that Mel-
bourne belledom took so frantically to these
florified night-caps last summer, when pursuing
out-door pastimes, that grave doubts were enter-
tained as to whether their ears weren’t growing
too largo for the erstwhile übiquitous “sailor”
hat? Young men objected in vain that they could
only see a frill and a bow for Angelina’s side-view
at the tennis-courts, but their moans met with
silent contempt, and the sun-bonnet reigned
triumphantly.
As a Darwinesque sort of theory, the notion
that woman is descended from a parrot wouldn’t
answer badly if intelligently argued Out. For
example, I sat near a girl the other night who
looked as if she would say “Pretty Joey ” if you
scratched her poll. Her garment was cheese-
yellow with red poppies on either shoulder, and
daring dabs of green, and startling lapses into
black. And all that parrokeetishness was sur-
mounted by a nose like a fish-hook and eyes
circled 4 'bird-wise with black rings ! I know it is
good taste to dress to suit personal idiosyncracy,
but at the same time a woman with a beak shaped
nose oughtn’t to wear a sulphhr-coloured feather
in her hair to make herself more like a cockatoo.
You might as well say that the husky lady who
weighs 15st. ought always to dress in elephant’s
breath !
Which reminds me that a woman once wrote to
me, explaining that her well-grown and attractive
daughter, whose appearance had made somewhat
of a sensation on the Block, wore skirts to her
knees and a big sun-bonnet as a cure for sunstroke.
I have pined ever since to know whether it was
the bare supports or the frilled head-gear that con-
stituted tire specific ? If Melbourne belledom
shortens its skirts to burlesque-point next summer
and still sticks to the sun-bonnet, it will seem to
argue that the calves have it.
A certain magnate hasn’t got a very winning
way with him—obviously hasn’t come out to make
himself popular. For instance, an ex-lieutenant,
who was hopelessly on His beam-ends, the other
day wrote to His Ex., asking him, for the sake of
the service they had mutually adorned, to give
him some assistance. And did that Governor
thrill with old reminiscences of camaraderie and
send him a bawbee or two? Not quite. The vice-
regal reply was a couple of soup-kitchen tickets
per medium of the Charity Organisation Secre-
tary !
Fillis’s Circus rnatineed last Saturday, and I
want to suggest that the shapely ladies who hung
by one eyelash from the top of the tent might put
on a little more sleeve when performing by day
than they do by night. I allude mqre particularly
to the black-and-yellow seraphs who hang on high
like a couple of wasps, and not to the pale-pink
lady in a crimson doublet slashed with blue and
silver, whose attire would do credit to the most
discreet tobacco-box. One plucky equestrienne
was hideously dressed in a white kilt and panta-
loons such as one’s grandmother wore as a 'child,
culminating in rose-pink stockings ; also, her hair
was hanging loose, and yet her arab steecDnever
shied at her ! lam led to believe that this get-up
is a copy, in circus colours, of the attire a woman
who straddles her mount usually adopts, and, if
so, I have no fear that cross-riding will ever
become a matter of choice in Australia.

wood.” By this marriage the lady, it is said,

A revised version of “Hans the Boatman”
was well received at Sydney Criterion on Saturday
night. “ Hans ”is a dramatic mixture, largely
dependent on the name-part, and a play that it is
possible for one man (and a dog) to haul along to
fame is always sure to hit the mark, provided you
have a star in the lead. Charles Arnold plays
Hans as if the_ melodies and vagaries of that
attractive Arcadian came natural to him, and is
excellently supported by the taking Henrietta
Watson in the ungrateful part of Gladys.
» • •
Dob Frederics makes a militant and inex-
haustible Jeffie in green stockings and red hair.
Her singing-voice is a reminiscence of Alice
Leamar, only that A.L. wouldn’t have sung so
sickly a ballad as “The Spirit>of the Lake,” even
if the management had gone on its knees to her.
Dot Frederics’ clear and' confidential soprano
would find its best opportunity in something like
“ Whacky-whack.” Little Jessie Mead, the small
Hans and the fetching, but rather over-done, Baby
Coquetth, deserve the fearful term of “ infant
prodigy,” and “ Nord ”, stars as usual. By the
way, the baby-coquette oughtn’t to ask Hans to
sit on her knee. It is a false note and spoils the
whole scene.
* • •
One striking novelty about “Hans the
Boatman ’ is .the attire of the villainy Darrell
Vincent. It is generally conceded to be impos-
sible to make hay 'of the decalogue on the stage in
anything short of a tall, shiny hat and patent-
leather boots, bat the only suggestion of iniquity
about Albert 'Lucas is a cigarette; and,that has
an uphill fight to imply cold and calculating
depravity against the virtuous 'influence of a hard
brown hat and' a meek turn-down collar. The
mission in life of Mr. Lucas is evidently to be a
stage detective, with occasional lapses into doing
odd jobs for the villain. Frank Cates, as the
spoony lieutenant, only leaves the impression that
he wants hi.s hair out; and Lachlan M‘Gowan,
as the belligerent boatman, and George Lash
Gordon, as the petrified parent, throw no new
light on these people. A flourishing baby-farm is
suggested by the rest of the caste.
** * *
Business fell off badly at Sydney Lyceum
in the last days of “Jo.” But the record for un-
precedented badness was readied when “The
Grasshopper” took the stage on Saturday night.
The big theatre never struck such a patch of utter
collapse and desolation before. * The Grass-
hopper ” is the worst thing of its kind The Bulle-
tin remembers. It almost bdongs to the crude
variety of comedy which an infant perpetrates
with an inky thumb-nail in a copy-book, and it
contains about every kind and varietyof foolishness
which could be compressed intb the space. There
are many old motherless jokes in it, and the original
pun which was saved in the Ark has been disinterred
frorn its well-earned rest, and carefully inserted as
a 'thing to be proud of. Bright, little Jennie Lee
keeps “The Grasshopper ” afloat, and is the sole
support of an utterly undeserving production.
Why this clever lady chooses to waste
her ability upon it is a mystery which
is never likely to be solved. Saturday night’s
audience took “ The Grasshopper ” in profound
and smileless silence, but it did its beat to cheer
the leading lady to the echo. Only there wasn’t
enough of it to make an echo, and the general
effect was that of one cockroach applauding in a
b L rn ‘ (Thursday) is the! vst of the season,
when Miss Lee bids a long iarewell to Aus-
tralia. It ought to be a great, warm, enthusiastic
occasion with a dense crowd standing on each
others’ heads, but the shadow of “ The Grass-
hopper ” is over it, and Jennie’s farewell will
probably be about the saddest thing that ever
happened to her.

Sydney Royal, which has lately developed
the shutting-up disease in an agggravated form,
closed some more last week ainid considerable con-
fusion. Du Monday night most of the orchestra
was visibly on strike, and the balance wore an
aggravated expression. Also, the co. had thinned
out considerably, and what remained of it could
be heard arguing behind the curtain. On Tues-
day there was.no oreheatra at all and no co., and,
after some feeble attempts at explanation, the
audience demanded its njioney back. Then there
was the usual desire to break things ; the police
came along, and proceedings closed with the
accustomed objurgations on the pavement. The
Royal has lately struck a patch of tolerably hard
luck, and unless Maggie Moore returns its pro-
spects seem rather desolate.
George Rignold’s Anglo-Irish drama is
doing- such good business that the next outbreak
°f “Lights of London” has been postponed in-
definitely. _ This is a bad time for everything,
more especially for six-aot melodrama, but Henry
V. keeps his big show going through good and
bad fortune.
«« m «
Good old S,t. Patrick’s annual festival, last
Saturday, was celebrated at Sydney Royal with
the‘‘Colleen Bawn.” The performance was for
the benefit of Messrs. Arthur Vivian and Tom
King, but the former, owing to a severe lameness
in the throat, could not appear, and the drama
was thus shorn of some of its glories. Also, the
audience was much thinner than the show
deserved, partly through a general lack of interest
in Sb. Patrick, partly because the “Colleen” is
not what it was, and partly because the curse of
the Royal lies heavily on the old house. The
worm that dieth not was in the takings, and
Arthur Vivian, an excellent comedian, who has
drifted by mistake into the wastes of gory melo-
drama, deserved better fortune.
«* * *
Fillis’s Circus opened its Sydney season
with the biggest crowd that the huge tent could!
hold, and there is every sign of a continuous boom.
The circus is very much the same as it was on its
last visit, bub even if it was a great deal more so
it would probably boom just the same. There is
a vast amount of it for the money, and most of it
is good. Perhaps the best feature is the Feeley
family of acrobats—a combination which will
assuredly break its neck someday. The “stag-
hunt,” too, seems to have caught on, and the other
items are feeling pretty well.
** * *
The N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s show
will be opened at noon to-day (Thursday) by the
Governor. A luncheon follows. The live stock
entries are more numerous than ever before, and
the exhibition will be unusually attractive and
complete.

»* * *
“ La Mascot-te,” at Melbourne Princess’s,
has probably caused a slight fall in the financial
barometer. People are beginning to lose faith in
this tadpole opera, with its large, round head and
thin, tapering tail. Audran’s charming music is
not as young as it was., also the best of it has
been heard by 9.20 p.m., and when the plot loses
all comedy interest, which happens before the
close of the second act, nothing remains for “ La
Mascotte” but to file its artistic schedule. The
show has gone bung. A wild pas de trods in the
last act tries to hide the real state of affairs, but
the restoration of public confidence is only fpr a
moment. Rocoo and his royal master throw in a
new gag or two every night and play like true
comedians as long as there is airy cqmedy to deal
with. The musical numbers are made the very
most of, the pages who propose to raffle Bettina
within the precincts of tire court are gilt-edged
pages, and many other matters are unexception-
able. Nevertheless, Melbourne will not rend its
garments when the opera retires this (Thursday)
evening.

“ Paul Jones,” a work to memory dear,
will inaugurate ,a deadly struggle between old and

Stewart, of the original cast, is to have Ryley for
her bold buccaneer, whilst some other members
of the Princess’ company should.be well placed.
** * *
At the Royal an exuberantly Milesian
audience achieved a record attendance last Satur-
day night. The house was crammed everywhere,
and this for a performance of Boucicault’s oldest
and far from best of Irish dramas. Maggie Moore
and company don’t give a great account of them-
selves in “The Colleen Bawn.” The Colleen is
not sufficiently demonstrative to suit a bouncing
patriotic actress like Miss -Maggie. When she
throws off her artless simplicity and gives them the
“Wearing of the Green” the effect is incon-
gruous. Miss Deorwyn and Stewart, junr., made
a commendable second pair of lovers, H. E.
Roberts is a pleasant Myles, whilst J. P. West
overacts the part of Danny in a promising manner.
But the roar of Augustus Glover and the make-up
of John lord©are sad features of a generally dis-
appointing entertainment. Easter Saturday at
the Royal is fixed as the date of the “ Struck Oil ”
revival. Advertisements speak of “Sir John
Madden, LL.D,, having Refused the Application
°f J- O. Wilhamgon to stop the Production of
‘Struck Oil.’” Which doesn’t seem quite a fair
announcement, seeing that the application wasn’t
carried along to the bitter end, nor did the Yic-
torian-Irish Judge do more than refuse to stop
the play from being advertised in advance.
However, all s fair in theatrical business, per-
haps. Maggie Moore, whose historical perform-
ance of Lizzie. Stofel needed no law-wrangle
to boom it, has been considerably boomed all the
same, and “ Struck Oil ” promises to go big.
*« * *
The halls are in a state of Easter excite
ment, too. the little Victoria is about to re-open,
after a long vacation, under the style of the
Alcazar. Coghill Bros, are entering into posses-
sion of the Gaiety, where they will “adhere to
their old motto, Refinement,” and the-Alhambra
is preparing a fresh programme to cope with these
rival outbreaks. Melbourne is unduly busy in
the show line, and somebody is certain to feel
sorry shortly.
»* * *
“ The Shaughran,” as set up last Saturday
at the Alexandra, is so much better than several
previous renderings of the same drama at higher
prices that it ought, by comparison, to run for
three months. Measured by the tariff of charges
for admission this is a colossal triumph. Even
the fortress, which is expected to go wrong on its
pivot at a first performance, accomplished a
peaceful revolution, and the venerable mechanical
change, that has been starred in a thousand bills,
came off absolutely without a hitch. Dan Barry,
being Irish by profession and alive to the fit-
ness of things where the ould sad is con-
cerned, has inflated the company with a
special engagement of Martyn Hagan. Hagan
brings a good brogue and a lively style into
the name - part, and his humour is consider-
able. Miss Edith Appleton is the sweetest little
Moya seen in Melbourne since Nina Boucicault


DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHA.T IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can bo
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling prices for the best is eightpenoe, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpenoe, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you 7 Will you do your business at

** * *
Miss Lalla Miranda, who is about to fly to
Europe, will take with her the best wishes of
Australia’s concert-going public. To hear this
gifted young vocalist tune her lay, in the sweetest
and most flexible of light soprano voices, is only a
little more delightful than to see her perform the
task. Her platform stylo, as pretty as her name,
always gave promise of that acting ability which
was lately shown in opera performances at Mel-
bourne Exhibition. The daughter of a well-known
teacher of music and singing, Lalla Miranda was
born into her profession, and made her first ap-
pearances at an age when most damsels are
wrestling with the rudiments of musical educa-
tion. Six years ago, if we remember rightly, she
carried off first prize in a singing competition for
“juveniles” of sixteen. Miss Miranda, anyway,
is very young and very charming. She warbles
like the nightingale, and is otherwise deserving of
the success which lies before her.
Miss LALLA MIRANDA,
At Poverty Point.

boards. We can give chapter and verse—but no matter.

went to a newspaper office to insert a few lines in memory
of his father, and, asking the charge, was told 2s. 6d. per
inch, “ Great Scott! I can’t afford it—my father was six-
foot-two.”

Dear Bulletin, —A suggestion. One by one our bright
partieular stars take the long journey northward to shine
in other worlds than ours. Melba, Frances Saville, Pattie
Browne have gone, so have the two Cunninghams and
Morton. Ada Crossley goes this month; Lalla Miranda
follows, and it is also on the sards that Mary Weir and
Violet Varley go shortly. They are. all most like]y lost to
us. Now, instead of letting our operatic treasure dribble
away in drops like this, why does pot soiqe enterprising
and speculative syndicate—“the Firm” are enterprising
—take to Britain and America a completely-furnished
opera co., just to show J. Bull and Brother Jonathan that
Australia can do more than turn out cricketers, oarsmen,
and pugilists ? There’s money in the idea. JUst tell ms
whether, from the following list, you could not select a
complete comic opera co. equal to any in London or the
States: Nellie Stewart, Frances Saville, Colbourne Baber,
Flo Graupner, VI Varley, Ida Osborne, Florence Young,
May Pollard, Pattie Browne, Dooy Stewart, Male Saqui,
Mary Weir and the eight Princess Ballerinas; Roland
Cunningham, Charlie Harding, Syd. Deane, Howard
Vernon, Tom Grundy, Billy D’Ensem, Will Xewers.—
Yours, Steele Grey.
The cast of “The Amazons,” to be staged by the
B. and B. 00. on Saturday, at Sydney Lyceum, will be
practically the same as that of the former Criterion per-
formance. Miss Major will play the part of Sergeant
Shuler, but otherwise there is no change. That beautiful
and successful idiot, Lord Tweenwayes, will be repre-
sented again by Cecil Ward, who has. already made him
the' most celebrated ass who ever struck this country ;
and Boucicault’s Frenchman—the most perfect character
of its kind The Bulletin remembers—couldn’t be altered
except for the worse. Likewise the athletic Edith Blande,
the only actress in Australia who could possibly look like
the mother of the boy-girls, is again to appear in that
capacity.

as man and wife in August.

At Adelaide Cycloratna, a neat illusion, the figure of
Napoleon stalking the field of Waterloo, has been intro-
duced. Napoleon is impersonated by one J. B. Morley,
said to be a nephew of John Morley, the Irish Chief Sec.
The resemblance, both as to face and figure, is most
remarkable.
The hysterics of two fairly well-known Sydney song-
stresses now on tour with an opera eo. in the back blocks,
recall Rudyard K.’s description of hysterics in a girl’s
school. First the contralto started to drum on the floor
with her heels, and then the golden-haired soprano
followed suit from sheer sympathy—upon which scene
the distracted male management lowered the curtain for
fear the infection would spread orer the house. Kipling
explains how tt goes from girl to girl in “ Soldiers
Three.”

Mr. Justice Hood’s recent remarks upon lawyers’
money transactions, and his doctrine of legal morality
therein to be found, is a roundabout reminderthat Matthew
Davies belongs to the legal “push,’ and that other lawyers
were also implicated in certain Melbourne companies
whose directors have thus far escaped the severer forms of
trouble.

A case is down on the N.S.W. Full Court list
by which it is proposed to settle, as a matter of dry law,
the question wheth r the Northern Associated Collieries
Association is not an illegal association, on the eround
that it is “in restraint of trade.” Trades-unionists and
Labour politicians should follow the legal arguments with
interest. The disputed point may cut several ways.

“ I was in Melbourne all the afternoon of the
day on which the Mercantile Bank prosecutions ended,”
writes a correspondent, “and only heard one newsboy cry
the fact. The interest in it was as dead as the land-boom
it resulted from, and the verdict was a foregone conclu-
sion.”

Apropos the Melb. municipal electric-light. It

• * * *
The latest Sydney dodge is for a prominent in-
solvent, running a Sydney “commercial” business, to
describe himself in his schedule as (say) “X. Smith,
grazier, of VVoollahra,” instead of (say) "X. Smith, com-
mission-agent.” The daily press lets it go as “grazier”
and makes no comments, and so hardly anybody knows
that Mr. X. Smith, of the well-known firm of agents,
X. Smith and Son, has “gone up.”

Jersey on the London board of his Oremorne (Port Jack-
son) Coal Mining Co. It is also stated that Panmuro

By the way, it would be interesting to look up the exact
text of some of the adulatory speeches concerning Gov.
Jersey delivered by Tiger just before the amiable Jersey’s
departure. In the light of present events, they are cal-
culated to make one think hard.

♦ « » *
The Standard Bank of British South Africa
doesn’t pay interest on fixed deposits. For every £4O
deposited in the bank the depositor'is charged 15s .per
quarter for"’the trouble of looking after it. Some Irish
banks do this also, but their charges are not so stiff and
vary according to the trouble incurred. However, the
money is there when you want it. From an Australian
point of view, all things taken into consideration, the
system has its advantages.
* * * *
Late Southern events have emboldened the
Smith-Howe-Abigail sympathisers to try and get their
gang out of gaol. The ruin they wrought is already for-
gotten, and 99 N.S. Wales M.’sP., headed by the Speaker,
sigh a petition for release 1 The verdicts which gaoled
these people were about the only sweet spots in the
financial rottenness of the last five years, and nothing
could more conclusively show than does this petition how
low are the existing standards of Parliamentary morality.
Meantime, the brutal Sikes languishes uncared-for.

think the municipality could meet its engagements.
Anybody who knows, has heard, or has dreamt of cooler

One considerable shareholder in the deceased
Federal Bank was four months old when he bought his
shares, and another was aged five years. It is alleged
