Libel law comes in again over the Investors’
Review, and Bank of England. Wilson, with his handful
of truth, could only open his little fingers, and then comes
a chorus of denial, from the venal, or ignorant, press.
Now it is evident he knew all about May’s quarter-of-a-
million, and the hushing up of the Times.
• * • •
Melbourne banks are now loaded up with un-
saleable city and suburban property, left on their hands
by borrowers, who found it cheaper to let it go than to
pay up the advances, to the nominal value of nearly
£20,000,000. It will, doubtless, realise that amount, or
even more, some day, but then no one knows when that
day will be. At forced sale just now it would probably
fetch £5,000,000, and, as that would mean general insol-
vency, Giere is nothing for itjmt to hold on. The situa-
tion is exactly that of the mail’ who holds a wild bull by
the tail: if he can keep the tail from slipping till the
animal dies of old age he is all right, but if he can’t then
it is all up with him. Meanwhile, when bank shares, 60s.
paid-up, are selling at 55., it means that the public con-
siders there is just one chance in twelve that the tail
doesn’t slip.
♦ * * *
The Q.N. Bank’s shares (70s. paid up) have
been on offer in Sydney at 2s. 6d., with no buyers.
• » » *
lb is interesting to learn that “ financial circles”
in Melbourne strongly disapprove of the Government
borrowing money locally at 4 per cent, and getting it At
par, instead of floating loans at a serious discount in
London. “Financial circles” consist mostly of recon-
structed bankers, and these persons realise that if the
only safe investment going can he shut up, the public
must trust its money again to the old shaky institutions,
which otherwise it would much prefer to leave alone.
Therefore “financial circles” are solid on the fact that
local borrowing is a curse, and that British borrowing is
the only genuine article.
* » ♦ ♦
Some of the Australian banks have the bump
of gratitude largely developed. In many cases it hap-
pened that the same customer had a large fixed deposit
and a good-sjzed overdraft on current account. When
the deposit was safely reconstructed for eight years or so,
it demanded, with a piratical aspect, immediate pay-
ment of the overdraft. The customer, who couldn’t pay,
generally tendered his deposit-receipt as security. Some-
times it was accepted; but one bank flatly refuses to
regard its own promise to pay as any security at all. The
best it will do is to buy back its own deposit at about
15s. or 16s. In the £, and pay itself the overdraft out of
the proceeds, and if this offer isn’t accepted it sells the
customer up.
« * * *
R. Hope Atkinson, a gentleman in the
mysterious “ property distribution ” business, was
arrested last week in Sydney for unlawfully running a
lottery. Considering, however-, that the Atkinson lottery
has been running in its own fitful, unsatisfactory way
for a year or more, there is something wildly humorous
about arresting the promoter now for his unlawful
gamble, which is no more unlawful to-day than it was 12
months ago. If common report goes for anything the
detective force has had its eye on the affair for many
months past; and why nothing came of it until now is a
mystery.
• « * *
Mr. Bulletin, — Tojgive you an idea of the
state of unemployed miners at Broken Hill. If a man
wants a job at the big mine he goes to see the manager,
who tells him to go to the foremen, who tell him to call at
the mine-office. We go there in the morning 100 strong,
and stand like cattle in a drafting-yard. By and by the
window opens, and Capt. Williams puts his head out and
phints his finger at the biggest man, with all his Sunday
clothes on and no sign of lead on his face—-most likely a
new arrival. There is work for him, and the next, and
the next; but those most in want of work—little men
with bad clothes and large families—cannot get it. It is
very disappointing to walk home day after day, week
after week, and month after month, and hear the wife say,
No luck again, Bill 1” Mr. Bulletin, I am a little man
with a big- family and a lot of lead on my'face, and my
clothes are worn out looking for w.ork. Will you ask
Capt. Williams to give us a show T—l am, A Respectful
Unemployed.

Halley, inventor of the percussion concentrating
table used a"t nearly every battery at Bendigo as well a.s
throughout all Australia, died the other day, aged 78.
The patent ran out this season but, just ere dying, he
patented an improvement of the cheapest, if not of the
best, concentrator used in Australia.

X). D. Rosewarne, a well-known mining man,
writes from London in opposition to the proposal of S.A.
Govt, to erect cyanide works on the ground that cyanide
is not a universal gold-solvent; that it was njade for the
Randt gold, which is the finest jn the world, and the per-
centage of gold saved there by the two processes, amal-
gamation and cyaniding, is not equal to that saved else-
where by amalgamation alone.

Another creep at Broken Hill—on the South
Central boundary, not on the Proprietary this time.
Slight trouble arose at this point two years ago. Being
on the^ boundary each manager scornfully points to ths
other man’s defective timbering as the cause.
South directors point out that the fall in silver
affects the mine little. Sulphide concentrates, the mine’s
sole output at present, contain only 9oz. to lloz. silver
per ton ; Id. per ounce silver fall, therefore, cannot in-
volve a difference of more than 9d. or lid. per ton.
Under present methods of disposal the 00. gets nothing for
this silver.

Recent Waukaringa (S. A.) finds have produced
the ghost of a gold-boom at Broken Hill. Someone was
seen carrying a peg recently ; same day 40 claims were
pegged out, and not a colour has yet been found in one of
them. The likeliest claim has a shaft which has been for
years the defunct-goat repository of the neighbourhood
and'must possess a large deposit (of bone-dust material)
by now.
• • • •
Broken Hill, says a recent visitor, was named
by people with prophetic vision. At present the town
carries more broken-down mining sharks to the acre than
any other mining town in Australia. Those who were
able have long ago fled to W.A., and the hopelessly dead-
beat section remains hanging on in the hope of something
turning up—4o spiders to one fly.

B.H. Proprietary run, last week, all but a

ore. Silver now stands at about 2s. 3Jd.
* « « •
The Bulletin hopes tose© the N.S.W. Minister
for Mines set his face like a flint against leasing at
Wyalong, and compel every holder of ground to work it,
under pain of forfeiture, it is said that a petition, signed
by 500 would-be leaseholders, has been sent to head-
quarters. So far, the gold appears to have been found
solely on conditional purchases or conditional leases,
which cannot be converted into mining leases, and the
result is that the claims are held as blocks by men who
really mean work. It is the public interest to tide as
many small and independent labourers over the winter as
the field will carry.
» « • *
Total Wyalong to date are 4740 z. from
173 tons, Supplied by 11 claims—a good, but not a brilliant
average for “a poor man’s field.” Wyalong has this in
its favour, however, that it’s mostly pick and shovel
work, and latest impressions seem to show that
the gold-bearing area is much more extensive than
originally supposed. Yet the 3000 men now on the field,
“and crowds arriving,” will evidently have to struggle
for tucker, and as there is no available work on wages,
men must go prepared to feed themselves until they turn
something up.

Great rains have fallen at Coolgardie — that is,
great for Coolgardie, though under a quarter-inch alto-
gether. Siberia was the only place which didn’t get any,
and Southern Cross had nearly an inch. The outside dis-
tricts got up to 2in. But the miners have only fallen
from thirst into famine. Population haying grown faster
than transport, provisions are running short: tinned meat,
4s. to 6s. per lb., and flour £6 per 601 b., and unobtainable
at that.

The W.A. water conservation-department, is
delighted with the success of its new plan for utilising
great rocks in that weary land. It runs a wall round the
top of them to serve as a'spouting, and saves alt the water
which falls in their catchment-area.

* * * »
W.A. discoveries of big surface patches of reef
gold recall the big find of Dr. Kerr at the Turon in 1851.
Kerr, who was related by marriage to the Suitor family,
had an aboriginal in his employ who was out in the bush
one day minding 'sheep, and he discovered some gold
quartz sticking out of the ground. He informed his
master, who rode out to the spot and unearthed a block
about a foot in diameter weighing 751bs. gross, out of
which over 60Ibs. pure gold was taken. Two other blocks
yielded 311 bs. gold. Dr. Machattie purchased the gold
at £3 7s. 6d. per ounce, the total coming to £4160. The
hundredweight of gold afterwards passed into the hands of
Messrs. Thacker and Co., and was then seized by Govt, on
the plea that it was found on Crown land by an unlicensed
geßson and a certain percentage of it belonged to the
rown. It was returned, however, in a short time, less
£4O for its escort frotii Bathurst to Sydney.

Evangelist Moody has heart-disease, and
his friends are sad. Why ? He is pre-eminently
fit for Heaven. But it is always the biggest saints
who are sorriest to quit.
The mystery of the Democratic ex-Attorney
General (S. A.) Homburg’s autocratic actions and
utterances is now solved. He and the Czar were
born on the same day—loth March.

orime under heaven bar one—suicide.”

and below the belt.” An interesting anatomical

Dr. Renwick—still plain Mister—is back
in Sydney from Chicago and London, with the
brand of the Colonial Office visible on his clothes,
and “next 24th of May ” shining like a benedic-
tion in his face.
The chief reason of Grey’s visit to England
—quite an undertaking for a man of his years—
is said to be his desire for a reconciliation with
his wife, from whom he has been estranged for
nearly 40 years, A pathetic errand, truly.
The 21st inst. (March) will be the 28th
anniversary of the pronouncing of the death-
sentence on murderer Bertrand at Sydney.
Bertrand is probably a record “lifer” in the
criminal history of English-speaking nations.
Cecil Rhodes (the man “ with the face of
a Csesar, the ambition of a Loyola, and the wealth
of a Croesus ”) has raised misogyny to a science.
Not only do he and his millions—fifteen or so—
resist the blandishments of every marriageable
woman in S. Africa, but all the members of his
personal and domestic staff are single. Anybody
in his service who gets married gets dismissed as
well. “ Marriage spoils a man or woman’s career
—destroys singleness of aim,” is his dictum.
Monsieur Alexis de Pon-
tiata, the new Russian Consul
in Melbourne, shrugs his shoul-
ders at the prevalent notion that
he is a diplomatist of the deepest
dye, having secret papers con-
cealed under the lining of his
hat. When a Bulletin am-
bassador first waited upon him
the consul was, in point of fact,
plotting the final details of a
lobster salad, or something, in
a dark corner of his outer office,
and a general impression of
glassware and cut flowers sug-
gested conspiracy d, la fourchette.
M. de Pontiatais a short, slight,
essentially Russian - looking
stranger. His large, oval Asiatic
eyes betray none of the fury of
his appalling ancestor, who
slaughtered the pagans of Nov-
gorod some nine centuries back.
The consul’s hair is thinning
rapidly on the top in a harmless
Australian manner, and a ten-
dency to bristle in his Russian
moustache is perhaps the only
suspicious circumstance about
him. A Greek and Latin scholar,
a considerable linguist and a
student of things in general, M.
de Pontiata’s first reason for
accepting his present appoint-
ment lay in his natural ambition
to produce eventually a standard
work on Australia for the benefit
of Russian readers. He has
already recommended the des-
patch of Russian samples to the
little Hobart Exhibition, and
has obtained a peck or two of
Eucalypti seeds from Baron Von
Mueller, being convinced that
the gum-tree will do well in the
Caucasian region. He is a
strong supporter of red-gum
blocks for street paving, and his
opinion of the Bulletin is ex-
pressed by a slight upraising of
the hands, followed by'a peculiar
smile.

away from home. His relatives determined to

remarked at a Melb. temperance garden-party
that “ nobody realises more than Lady Wrixon
the evils of immoderate drinking,” he little thought
he was perpetrating a standard joke for all the
English-speaking people. It has even got into the
French papers.

said : “Your Worship, having now disposed of

That Wesleyan Conference resolution
censuring “trading ” clergymen was probably
directed at Fitchett, whose literary gifts the
Church pooh-poohed at first. His gossipy
“X.Y.Z.” was the feature of the Spectator, but
the directors let him slip, on a paltry question of
payment, and he was lost to the sect as Southern
Cross editor.
“ Marriage is a lottery.” There lately lived
in a Melbourne suburb a woman who, many years
ago, in Ballarat’s “golden days,” put herself up
to be raffled for—tickets <£s apiece. She was
handsome, and tickets sold readily. The raffle
duly came off, and the winner at once married his
prize. They lived happily together for many
years, and she was then left a widow.
The Empress Eugenie has practically
adopted the illegitimate son of the late Prince
Imperial by the daughter of a Surrey Park
ranger. The boy, who is often seen about arm-in
arm with the ex-Empress, is now about 14, and is
described as a lightly-built young fellow, tall and
well-shaped, and very like his father. His mother
lived in Melbourne some years after his birth.
The Moderator of Sydney Presbyterian
Assembly popped up last week in a regalia, com-
prising a braided coat “trimmed with the finest
lace,” knee-breeches of black cloth, black silk
stockings, and silver buckled shoes. The brethren
promptly voted a further honorarium of ,£3O to
the Moderator in consideration of his expensive
clothes, and the meeting asked Heaven’s blessing
upon the holy show.

Dear Bulletin,— The theory that “fat is
force ”is weak in the knees. Christ disproves it,
also Mahomet. Take the world’s three greatest
statesmen—Alexander, Csesar, and Napoleon ; the
first died at 32, and before he had had time to
grow fat; the leanness of the second amounted
almost to a disease, and the star of the last-named
only commenced to wane as his adipose tissue
began to accumulate. Parnell and Gladstone, the
two greatest commoners of the century, were both
lean men. So were Robespierre, Desmoulins,
Washington, Lincoln, Windhorst, Cavour,
Disraeli, and John Russell; and so are Balfour,
Chamberlain, Davitt, John Dillon, and a host of
Mr. P. P. DAKIN.
others too numerous to mention. When Shake-
speare’s Csesar feared for his safety it was the
machinations of the lean men that he dreaded :
“Let me have men about me that are fat.” As a
general rule, you will find it is the “ lean, strong
man,” or often-as in the case of Robespierre and
Windhorst —the shrunken dwarf, that “shifts the
earth.”—D. W.
David -Gaunson, we see, though ranking
among lawyers as an attorney, wears wig and
gown in Court. It is sad, in view of Gaunson’s
present attitude, to muse on what he might have
been. Of old, he was a fiery Democrat, and a
heau-sabrcur in speech—the generous leader of
social forlorn hopes, the eloquent mouthpiece of
all who were trampled on, the incisive scorner of
the gilded vulgarians of politics and their allies in
the press. To day—alas ! he is chiefly known as
the hired professional advocate of busted boomers.
But the Democracy was most ungrateful to the
sensitive Gaunson.
A N.S.W. Judge, appealed to by a
certain newspaper for leave to publish the
evidence in a recent unsavory divorce-
suit, replied : “You should be ashamed of your-
self ; I was once a newspaper - man, but I
never helped blackguardism. Poor soul! go,
serve your time to a blacksmith, instead of hang-
ing around here to pick up dirty details of a dirty
case. Tell your editor from me that he is a social
disease. Against yourself, young man, I have
nothing; but surely you can pick pockets for a
living; well, that’s better—anything’s better than
what you’re at.”
Writes a Bulletin London correspondent:
When Ernest Giles, the explorer, came to England to
publish his book, “Australia Twice Traversed,” he
brought with him some fine specimens from, I think,
Dookie, and was followed by two tons of stuff from a
mine there, and a bag of rich specimens. Henniker
Heaton tried to get up a Carlton Club syndicate to exploit
the mine, but nothing resulted. The quartz was rich,
though showing no gold, for the result of the crushing at
Johnson and Matthey’s was over 30oz. to the ton, whilst
the picked specimens gave as high as 80oz. To the late
Charles Stewart Parnell, who was an expert assayer,
Heaton had given a small piece of the quartz Giles brought
in his portmanteau—it was grey-looking stuff, and gave
no indication of phenomenal richness—and this Parnell
tested. On the occasion of the fierce debate on the
Parnell commission, the great leader walked across the
floor of the House to Heaton, and pulling out of his waist-
coat pocket a piece of tissue-paper, handed it to him,
saying, “This is the gold I got from the piece of quartz
you gave me.” It was a small piece of gold, no bigger
than a pin s head, and with it was the result of his assay,
which agreed with Johnson’s bulk trial. And this in
the midst of a commotion which the House has rarely
witnessed. Such was Parnell’s sang froid.
Dear Bulletin, —The historical [lamented
H. Mortimer Franklyn, of Federal Australian
fame, was not a “blue-eyed Yankee.” He was a
grey-eyed Cockney. He certainly had some
singular experiences in the U..5.A., but he was
only a sojourner there as here, and there was
nothing of the “haw’ about him, although he
affected the “superior person,” the imperturbable
Vere de Vere sort of thing. All the same, he had
a devilish temper, which sometimes got loose.
The accountant was generally within reach and
got lots of the breeze when Morty was angry;
he was “ sacked ” on three separate occasions but
came back again the next morning in each case.
Franklyn made no comment on his reappearance,
that being one of his Vere de Vere peculiarities.
A cheap clerk came to him on one of these
occasions and offered to do the work for .£1 per
week /ess than the amount he was then paying.
Mortimer’s reply was to the effect that he thought
it would, in the long run, be cheaper to pay full
salary to the devil he knew. Perhaps the Melb.
man who tells the funny story about the “ Sul-
tan,” his “haw” style, &c., is the cheap man.
Franklyn has been bitterly condemned for his
financial exploits, yet he was a Bayard compared
to some of our truly pious boomers— en passant,
what a boomer was lost in him, thank heaven!
There is no man without his good points, and
Franklyn had plenty. He often did good turns,
and without fuss or afterthought. Temporarily
embarrassed or sick employes frequently got loans
or gifts from him. One noted sporting writer
was for months utterly prostrated by serious
illness, but he got his per week all the time.
As to his fortune, it is improbable he took much
wealth away from these shores; he was too
extravagant. Howbeit, an old Federal comp.,
returned from England, asserts that he saw in a
London paper that H. Mortimer Franklyn had
been left by his uncle a legacy of <£122,000. That
was Morty’s luck. Apropos, the present writer
was the dismissed accountant. —Yours truly, J.M.
Mr. Francis F. Dakin, the
present Y.R.C. handicapper, has
been identified with the horsey
history of Victoria from the day
of his landing, in 1871, when he
and his brother brought with them
The Marquis, a winner of the Two
Thousand Guineas and St. Leger
in England. As a trainer Mr.
Dakin made his first big mark in
1875 with Newminster, who pulled
off the Maribyrnong Plate, and at
this time he was in charge of
winners like Haricot and Sailor,
not to mention a horse bearing
the savoury name of Irish Stew.
With Darebin the subject of this
notice won the V.R.O. Derby in
1881, and the Adelaide Birthday
Cup a year later. On behalf of
Hon. William Pearson, of Long
Tunnel fame, Mr. Dakin pre-
pared good little Fryingpan (the
sire of Fortunatus) and the even
better Commotion for many of
their best victories. Another
notable horse in the Pearson
stable was Royal Oak, winner of
the G.N. Steeplechase in 1887, and
runner-up for the same event next
year. Mr. Dakin’s official connec-
tion with the V.R.C. began in
1889,_ when he took possession of
the judge’s box and gave those
solemn decisions from which there
is no appeal. Not long afterwards
he migrated to the position of
handicapper, vice Mr. Barnard,
and as handicapper he has pro-
bably come as near to perfection
as any keen student of “ form ” can ever
hope to get. Some of the handicap races
at last Cup meeting, for instance, were strik-
) n B> proofs of the care that had been taken
m bringing horses together. It is certain that
the recent charge of unfair weighting, brought
against Mr. Dakin by the “syndicate,” found
absolutely no support whatsoever from the public
who stand by and watch the game.
One of Victor Daley’s best stories relates
to a famous R. C. charity-sermon preacher who
was once brought along to the church where little
boy Victor served as an acolyte, and gambolled
innocently around the altar. The tabernacle re-
quired much repairs, and the famous “awakener”
was desired to give the congregation a “ regular
good rousing.” He did. Daley can’t speak of the
sermon even now without a shudder. The preacher
selected the “Worm that Never Dieth ” for his
texb He went right into the anatomy of the
horrid, crawling thing, dwelt upon its writhings
and twistings and eternal bitings at the innards of
those who wouldn’t give freely towards the new
r e°f-, Men groaned, boys turned pale, women
shrieked and tore their bonnets. Wben the audi-
ence was on the verge of a collapse from spiritual
funk, the preacher—he had been walking up and
down the altar district all the time—put his hand
before his mouth and whispered, **Go round with
the plate.” Thereupon Daley and the other col-
lector went round and took up an enormous sub-
scription, whilst the genius fired off his final par-
ticulars re the Worm. Presently the congregation
departed and the offertory was totted up. The
P. r T? c^er ’ mo PPing his wet brow, enquired casually,
How much ? ” They told him the amount —a
record achievement. “Ah ! ” said the holy father,
starting to dry his neck, “I have never known the
Worm to fail,”

FLETCHER’S PILLS
never fail to cure
INDIGESTION,
COSTIYENESS,
SOUR BREATH,
HEARTBURN,
LIVER DISEASE and
KIDNEY COMPLAINTS.
FLETCHER’S PILLS and CLEMENTS
TONIC are the recognised household remedies of
the Australian colonies, and every designing
quack tries to trade on their reputation and
renown. This is the greatest proof of thejr merit,
and sufferers want to be particular to get the
genuine articles, as regret and disappointment are
sure to follow the use of the thousands of “ All-
failing” remedies so freely advertised. The repu-
tation and wide-spread use of Clements Tonic and
Fletcher’s Pills are the greatest proofs of their
appreciation by the public. If they were not as
represented they would have passed out of
memory long ere this; but instead their sale is
greater and they are more esteemed day by day
and week by week, and this emphatically proves
their undisputed supremacy. Listen to no argu-
ment from interested parties ; demand the genuine
articles and TAKE NO SUBSTITUTE.

the S.A. Assembly is preparing to assail free edu-
cation

the Kiama ghost, calmly says he is going “to devote
the rest of his life to the union of the Australias.”
Every Australian politician who con-
spicuously loves power, seems to think himself
specially capable of personally managing the
military forces.

Meaning thereby for squelching Victorian
Liberalism by coalescing with Service ?
M‘llwraith tells Pall Mall Gazette he
would support the prohibition of Japanese labour
on the Q. sugar fields. The colour line again.
Mae. is an extremist. Black but no tan.
“ Interest eats day and night, and the more
it eats the hungrier it grows. The farmer in debt,
lying awake at night, can hear it gnaw his heart.
If he owes nothing he can hear the corn growing.”
—lngersoll.
Rev. W. G. Marsh, Anglican, after in-
specting the Vic. Village Settlements, publicly
denounces them as a mere shuffling with the
question of poverty 7. The people starve there
instead of in town, that is all, he says.
Patterson cables congratulations to Rose-
bery on his attaining the British Premiership.
An anonymous South Australian congratulated
Patterson when he became Premier. This wire
read : “ Congratulate you. Sympathise with the
country.”
Be Hoolan’s resignation
of his seat for Georgetown (Q.).
At <£3 a week there is nothing
in Parliament for a man whose
interests are all Northern, and
whose labour-principles leave no
show for loaves and fishes.
Lilley or Glassey is suggested
for the vacancy. It must be a
certainty before Lilley will
stand, for the old man’s pride
got a bad knock at North Bris-
bane, and he swears he’ll never
risk another
Some more of the bless-
ings of private enterprise and
the influx of British capital are
coming home to the people of
Westralia. That infatuated
province granted to the W.A.
Land Co. the right to select vast
areas of land at a few pence per
acre, and the bloated corpora-
tion proceeds to exercise its
right by taking up allotments
in new Government townships,
so that the State has the
pleasure of seeing town-lands,
which might have brought in
scores of pounds per acre, going
to the British capitalist for less
than as many coppers. The
matter was referred to the Privy
Council, and that body has
decided in favour of the co.,
“ with costs.” Probably all the
money which the Treasury will
ever get for its town-lands from
the W.A. Land Co. will fail to
pay those “costs,”
Suaviter in modo has super-
seded for titer in re as, a black-,
birding motto. The Kanaka-
catcher up- to. date has just
returned to Brisbane with his
phonograph and his magic-,
lantern, which helped to lure 88
recruits with carefully-selected
sounds and sights from Queens-
land sugar-plantations. . There
was no “ your ,
you —■ son of a con-
sumptive waster, get on with
that thrashing!” in the
phonograph. The charmed
islanders heard only “Please,
oblige me by proceeding with
the thrashing, Samuel,” and
similar courtesies.,. And the
hmelight always showed a. fat
Kanaka stuffing down roast-
pig; never, by any chance, a
thin wretch being kicked into
adding his tally to the biggest
death-rate in Australia. No,
sir.
Six hundred thousand women now in
excess in Great Britain. Something is coming—
perhaps polygamy.
Colonial Secretary Tozer (Q.), when in
Sydney lately, expressed the conviction that the
only way to deal with the unemployed was to
send them either on to State farms, at a low
weekly wage, or to hard labour in gaol, with a
wage attached.
One hundred and sixty temporary hands
had to leave the N.S.W. Electoral office in the
past fortnight. It is said that not one of the many
nominees of a certain North Shore politician, who
lately did the Ministry a valuable service,” was
among those dispensed with.
George Ash, M.P. (S.A.), chews the cud
reflectively whenever a fresh Village Settlement
is formed on the Murray. Ash was a strong
opponent from the first, and as the Murray is in
his district, the ex-jonrnalist may have to reckon
with 1000 angry electors at next S.A. elections.
The Tasmanian want-of-confidence fight,
which endedjn a two-vote Govt, majority, was a
mere party contest. Mr. Braddon, Imperialist,
Carlton Club man, knight, taking charge of the
so-called Democratic party in the House-of-
Assembly is surely a subject for savage derision.
Dear Bulletin, —When is pig-headed J.
Bull going to see the wolf at his door ? I came
across in my lodgings the other day a volume of
cheap photographic views of J. B,’s capital. The
work was entitled “The New Album of London,”
and bore the legend: “Made in Germany!”—
Paul Gibbs.
Rosebery has given substantial hostages to
the Radicals, if cablegrams are correct. Local
Option, One-man-one-vote, Scotch and Welsh
Disestablishment, Scottish Local Government,
Strike Conciliation Boards —how many score of
years before Gladstone would have got so far,
even in promises ?
The retiring Commissioners of the Vie.
Railways—they who came after Speight—have
issued a valedictory report which they could not
close “ without expressing thanks to members of
Parliament who have refrained from unduly
pressing the wants of their constituents.” What
a tribute to political virtue !
“Nationality is, in view of the uprising
host of the native-born population of Australasia,
inevitable and desirable. It will be the primary
fact and duty. It will be the irresistible outcome
of natural patriotism. To fight against it will be
to fight against Nature; and all such fights are
foredoomed to defeat.”— Brisbane Courier!
John Bright once called the Indian Civil
Service a “ huge system of out-door relief for the
aristocracy.” The Australian Civil Service is a
huge system of out-door relief for the privileged
classes. The Indian Civil Service was reformed
in the only way that of Australia can be—by the
open-competitive system of filling vacancies. Such
a system would help to kill Party Government.
Those monarchical Australian newspapers
that whooped so joyously about the insurrection
raised in Brazil by Di Mello, in favour of a
fallen imperial dynasty, are discreetly silent
anent the anti-republican sentiment with which
they alleged the Brazilians were saturated.
Except the Sydney Evening News which, as
usual, turns a spry somersault and cheers the dog
that has come out on top.
Matabeleland is finally absorbed in the
British lion’s greedy maw. It is to be governed
by the Chartered South Africa Co., while the
“ rights ” of the natives are relegated to a land-
commission nominated by Govt, and Co. The
natives had better have fallen before the Maxims
than endure such mercies. And now that the
country is “pacified,” “subscriptions are invited
in order to extend the blessed gospel to Matabele-
land.”

PARSON (to bush boy): “Well, Mike, my little boy, do you see
these things over there? (pointing to some tele-
graph-lines.)
MIKE : “Yes.”
P.: “ Now, what are they used for ? ”
M.: “ What are they used for? Why, just you shake a moke
and you’ll blessed soon see what the blanky blank
things are used for. My word ! ”

The Brazilian insurrection has flickered
out, and the republic will last a while longer.
The said insurrection was never much more
than a marine affair, for every time it got
a footing on land the gritty President Peixoto
headed it off, and' it had to take to the
ocean once more. There it led an unsatisfac-
tory existence on salt-junk for many days, but
all the while Peixoto was building, buying, and
arming ships in different parts of the world, and
at last he was able to corner the insurgents on
their own element. There was no fight then ; the
great insurrection simply doubled up.
Grey, who is an ardent Horae Ruler, will,
on his arrival in England, propound a scheme for
the settlement of the Irish question. And The
Bulletin will take a shade of odds—improbable
as it may now seem in view of the veteran’s age—
that before many months are over his voice will be
heard in the Imperial Parliament. Grey unsuc-
cessfully contested a British seat nearly 30 years
ago, and Carlyle, who never flattered anybody,
sent him a note the day before the election, as
follows “Let me know the result as soon as it
is over. It is the only contest I take any interest
in.” This, written in pencil, is in the Grey collec-
tion at the Auckland Free Public Library.
The irruption of English militaryism into
Australia resembles nothing so much as the whole-
sale emigration of Normans to England after the
Conquest, when
Robert of Coningby
Came out of Normandy
With his wife Tiffany,
And his maid Maupas,
And his dog Hardigras,
and his cat, and his cattle, and his household
deities and utilities, and everything else that was
Robert’s. Just so does Major-General Hutton
descend upon N.S.W. with a wife, three servants,
and baggage, and a bill of ,£316 2s. sd. In these
days of cheap fares the amount is undoubtedly
extravagant. But what does Parliament want?
It wants the imported militaire, and then grumbles
because he’s costly. Parliament must pay for its
whistle.
If The Bulletin were J. B. Patterson,
which it isn’t, and doesn’t want to be, it
would obstinately refuse, however frenzied
the public clamour, to call Parliament to-
gether before June. If we were Patterson we’d
reckon on being made a K.C.M.G. on Victoria’s
birthday next May, as an acknowledgment
of our “loyalty” in inviting the Yorks to Aus-
tralia. Further, we’d calculate on being sacked
instantly we met Parliament. If we were sacked
before the bauble came along and thus became
naught but a common member of the Opposition,
the bauble wouldn’t come at all, because, apart from
Patterson’s Premiership, which, under the afore-
said circumstances would be our Premiership,
there would be no excuse which could bear public
inspection for making us a K.C.M.G. Therefore,
though the heavens might fall in the interim, we
would clutch the degradation before we meet
Parliament, After that, the Deluge might follow
if it liked.
A fine sample of “ faked ” evidence. Be-
fore the Vic. Tariff Commission one firm stated,
as reported in the Sydney Telegraph’s news
columns, that they—
Had at one time employed 25.0 hands, but on account of
the high tariff and bad times they now employed only 16
men.
Then, in the “ brevities,” on front page of same
issue, the same story appeared in an improved
form:—
Evidence was given before the Tariff Board in Melbourne
yesterday, that owing to the high import duties the staff
of a carriage-builder had been reduced in a few years from
250 to 16 hands.
Here the “ bad times ” are entirely left out, and it
is all put down to the bless*ed tariff. Also, nothing
is said about four other carriage-makers who gave
evidence on the same day. One said that he
employed 115 hands; his business hadn’t fallen
off; and he was satisfied with the tariff. The
next two were also satisfied, and the remaining
one said that since the duties first began the price
of vehicles had gone down more than half. But
the good old Foreign-trade sup-press wasn’t lis-
tening just then, and all this evidence was left
out.
Mr. Henry Martin writes to the Tas. Demo-
crat to ask why The Bulletin doesn’t “ step for-
ward and advocate straight out the abolition of the
law which allows the recovery of interest by the
individual capitalist, and the substitution of a
law which shall make interest payable to the
State Bank,” which proceeding, he says, if
“ the interest so received by the State were
applied to pay the expenses of Government, would
be the means of really restoring confidence in
business undertakings; especially if the railways
arid other public departments were worked free of
charge to the public?” Well, one reason is that
The Bulletin doesn’t particularly want to
“restore confidence in business undertakings.”
Another is that such a device would burst
up every insurance and friendly society in the
land, and send many thousands of old men and
ancient widows and the like to the benevolent
asylum. A third reason is that there would
probably be a revolution, in the course of which
Mr. Henry Martin and The Bulletin would be
hanged on a lamp-post; and a fourth is that this
paper doesn’t want to see the railways worked
“free of charge” so that every Fat Man can
travel gratis, and have his wool and stores carried
free, and charge the expense to the taxpayer.
Also, there are several other reasons.
Hope for the future. The burning ques-
tion of to-day is Federation ! So all our great
men say. Admitted that its benefits would be
unsurpassed, why stand still like Balaam’s ass?
Take hope ! for there is comfort —yea —even to-
morrow—in a cup of “ Fry’s malted cocoa.”
Its strength, its pureness, its fame, its renown,
In the march of civilization is always to be found.
Shareholders in the Woggouora Building
Society will be cheered by the news that over six
inches of rain have fallen on their grazing farms
near Cunnamulla, Queensland, since 27th Feb., fill-
ing all the tanks, dams, and waterholes and ensuring
a good season. This society offers exceptional ad-
vantages to its members, as may be seen by its
detailed prospectus in these columns, and anyone
having £1 to invest would do well to consider this
opportunity of gaining a competency.*
“ All is lost, save honor.” — Fitchett on
Matthew Davies.
Photographic apparatus is being supplied
at Pentridge prison, which will take five different
portraits of the same prisoner at one time.
In a Tasmanian gaol lately a warder whose
duty it was to “clear” a time-expired prisoner,
took, instead of the right man, one who had 12
months to “ do,” and restoring to him his clothes,
&c., released him. When the mistake was dis-
covered, the police got on the track of the released
man and re-instated him.

DODD
T[?]
“TOMMY DODD,” the N.S.W. political agitator.
Prize-fights having been abolished, and
dog-fighting being illegal, also the Zoo officials
having decided not to let the Bengal tiger see the
other tiger, Sydney last week crammed the jury-
court to the doors to hear the malicious-prosecu-
tion action, John Norton, journalist, versus William
Patrick Crick, lawyer and M.P. John has a fine
command of all sorts of language, is a born con-
troversialist and fighter, and is pretty square in
the jaw. Add to this that he is a ready-witted
man of wide information and much platform ex-
perience, and a lovely hater; that he has a raucous
voice, a east-iron front, and carries enough fat to
give him staying power, and you have Mr. John
Norton, ex-editor of Truth, concerning the
business accounts of which all the trouble
arose. To give any particulars regarding
the quick-brained and aggressive, not to say
quarrelsome, Crick would' be to get pinched
for stale news. Norton conducted his own case
and was able to take all the (advantages of his
position, for before a jury it is, in a manner of
speaking, only the lay fool who is not his own
lawyer; he smiled derisively when defendant’s
counsel diplomatically asked the Court to
be as kind as it could be to the poor man
who was his own counsel. The end of the
row was a verdict for plaintiff for ,£750.
That night the Sydney public went to bed after
kneeling down and devoutly thanking heaven for
having preserved Crick and Norton as separate
entities instead of combining them into one politi-
cian and putting him into the Legislature. There
is always some small outside show for such people
as yearn to be in evidence while John and Patsy
remain at variance.
For a second time, James Martin and Co.,
the well-known Sydney agricultural engineers,
have secured the National prizes for implements
and machinery, and also for fruit-evaporators,
given by the New South Wales Government at
the Lismore show. They were also awarded the
Blue Ribbon for pumping appliances, corn
planters, corn-stalk cutters, harrows and cultiva-
tors, so it may be said that they have carried
everything before them.

Sociery
At Port Melbourne a man of 60 shoots
0r a Paltry grab at his eggs,
which killing is newspaper-trumpeted as a glorious
deed. We don’t envy him who more or less
wantonly slays a mere thief. If you are justified
in ki"i„g »for stealing pl.-Jor your
chickens, so is the Law.
* * *
While the Victorian Cabinet keeps up its
sanguine tone, it is plain that Melbourne poverty
is worse than ever. Here is one sign, which did
not appear even on the verge of last winter. In
the “ best ” suburbs, placards appear in the shop
windows, signed by locally-organised committees,
substantial men, not Premier Patterson’s bug-
bears, the “agitators.” One bill appeals to people
“ with any human feeling ” to contribute weekly
as much as they can spare, for the starving. Nor
are the recipients—the mediums—agitators, but
half-a-dozen of the most solid local traders.
* * »
A wife s letter to her husband, read in
N.S.W. Djvorce-court last week, concluded:
“Your loving wife, until divorce do us part.”
Judge Windeyer’s taste in jokes is distinctly grim,
and the thing made him smile.
. ’ ’ *. , ’
Another sample of Australian feminine divorce-
literature. Wife suing for divorce. Husband,
through solicitor, pleads for forgiveness: “Your
helping Mr. J to manage Yowlahbudgeree
station.” Wife, in rcoly :“ I shall not stay pro-
cee lings. I would not'again live with my husband
were he helping the Almighty to manage Heaven.”

Anent the recent elopement of a Tasmanian
girl with an elderly, widely-known, married
Lothario, Hobart is not so much shocked at the
mere act of elopement as at the point-blank refusal
of the lady to leave the object of her infatuation
and return to the paternal roof-tree. However,
sh s probably thinks she may as well be hanged for
a sheep as for a lamb, and in the circumstances
she is. from a merely “society” point of view,
perfectly right. ’

Many of the Australian
Supreme-Court Judges have
been so badly bitten by the
l oom and bank reconstruc-
tions that they are compelled
to heavily retrench, and in at
least one case, it is said, to
reconstruct. The Bulletin
lias long held a theory which
fh )uld apply to judges of all
kinds, including even P.M.’s.
No judge should be ap-
pointed whose actuarial ex-
pectancy of life is not equiva-
le.it to the tennhehas to serve
t >earn his pension. His life
s muld be insured for him by
Govt, out of his pay, aud the
poicy should be inviolable.
A Judge’s P.N. should be so
much waste-paper. He
should not be permitted to
hold any land except that on
which his home stands. He
should not belong to any
secret society, and should
hold no shares in any co.,
an! should be allowed no
investment except Govt,
b mds. Safety and placi-
dity should be imposed upon
him by Act of Parliament.
And if, when all this is
enforced, the judges ask for
more salary, let them have
it. Better this than to see a
ju Ige dispensing alleged jus-
tice with the fag-end of a
brain that has been worried
o’ nights over land-jobbing
concerns.

After the sickening mutual grease business
at Melb. Wesleyan Conference comes the follow-
ing from an Adelaide Church meeting. Dr. Torr,
the speaker quoted, is the head of Way College,
and was a State school-teacher till the Bible
Christians took him up and sent him to Oxford
at “ connexional ” expense, with a view to his
assuming his present position :
A friend of his said to him one day, “ Torr, how can you
work for a church which has so few men of talent among
her preachers? ” “ Talent,” he replied ;“ we have plenty
of talent. Have we not the warm sympathetic grip of a
Thorne, the crisp criticism of a Lake, the dissecting skill
of a Collins, the metaphysics of a Hancock, the research
of a Charles Martin, the evangelistic devotion of a
Schafer, the mathematical accuracy of a Richards, the
simplicity of a Dingle, the pastoral care of a Cann, the
unwearied pen of a James, the quiet unohlrusiveness of a
Lang, the sanctified common-sense of a Ready, the clear-
headedness of a Qratton, and the holy enthusiasm of a
Tressie?” (Loud cheers.) What more could he say ?
What more could he say—in the way of superla-
tive priggishness?

When first we join Life’s Carnival,
With Folly for a guide,
Beauty and Love and Luck are all
We want —the rest may slide.
But when we’re growing old and weak,
And groggy-legged and grey,
We’re thankful for a roof, and eke
Three certain meals a day.

“ Why don’t the unemployed go up the
country ?” One of them lately did so. He was a
first-class axeman and generally useful labourer,
and was anxious for work. He got a day’s em-
ployment from a parson at Y . The service
commenced at 9 a.m and concluded at 3 p.m.
During that time the son of toil cut up and
stacked three-quarters of a cord of wood and
thoroughly cleaned a set of buggy harness. When
benediction-time arrived the parson forked out
one shilling, and suggested that his drudge should
sweep the yard up a bit.
* * *
A busted boomster, some liar alleges 5
provides a fair amount of fun for those of his
unfortunate bank-depositors who belong to a cer-
tain suburban bowling-club. Way back in boom-
time, he gave a slice of landed estate to his club,
with the reservation that the title would be for-
feited if intoxicating drinks were ever sold on the
ground. For some while past the battered
Rechabite has taken a strange interest in bowls.

HEARD ON THE STREET.-A FACT.
FRIEND : “ Do you always have the nurse with you when you go
walking ? ”
STOUT WOMAN: “ Yes, indeed, I’m much too weak to be carrying
that great, heavy child about.”
He haunts his former property with the alleged
hope of bowling the management out in a
breakage of the liquor clause, and his ancient eye,
according to the scoffers, dwells lovingly upon
the 200 feet of green which would make such a
desirable site for villa residences. The cruel joke
of the situation lies in the behaviour of the poor
man’s former admirers. They gather round him
an d, producing flasks full of whisky-and-water
from their pockets, drink to the health of the
b . ai ? k - They pretend to hold spirituous orgies
right under his nose—and still the clause is being
duly carried out. In point of fact, the now-
beggared donor is the only fiery thing “ sold ” on
the bowling-green.

Much too big for their boots are those
N.S.W. Public-school potentates who haughtily
send home a shoeless child. When the State’s
educational intentions are thusmagnificently over-
ridden by the noble creatures whom abase material

The old question is being re-discussed—
AN UP-TO-DATE AMENDMENT.
DOT: “Give us this day our daily bread ”
MOTHER : “Go on, ‘ and for ’- ”
DOT: “And some more for the swagmen.”
“Are boots clothing?” Years ago the problem
was attacked in Queensland and Victoria, and
always answered in a way that permitted the
barefooted boy to attend school. An Under-
secretary once thus minuted a paper to his chief:
“If we demand boots, why not gloves?”
* * *
Another priest-story. First Friday in
Lent. Scene, dinner-table at Goulburn (N.S.W.)
hotel. Father O’Mulligan enters and quietly
takes seat opposite a well-known sportsman.
Sport, bent on a joke : “ Have a little chicken,
your reverence?” “Thank you, no, I have just
ordered a couple of eggs.” “Eggs be blowed; be
like me —a philosopher," pressing wing of fowl on
to the plate of his vis-a-vis. His reverence hands
wing to Newfoundland dog. Joker rises indig-
nantly ; “Sir, how dare you insult me!” “No
insult offered, sir,” replied the O’Mulligan, “I
merely wished to see whether my dog is a philoso-
pher or a Catholic, and I find he is a philosopher.’*

You can now get advice, with bottle and
medicine thrown in, from a duly qualified medical
man in Sydney (no quack) for two shillings. And
it pays, to the tune of about .£ISOO a year clear.
The average cost of bottle and medicine is
under 2d.

An interprovincial wire, headed “ Alleged
Unprofessional Conduct,” gives particulars of a
complaint against a solicitor that he had failed to
pay to a client a large sum collected on his client’s
behalf. “ Alleged unprofessional conduct !”
Alleged fiddlesticks ! Alleged larceny as a bailee !
* * •
N.S.W. Justice Windeyer is now following
his chief, Darley, in ordering that evidence in a
peculiarly nasty divorce-case be not published.
But why don’t they clear the court? When any
particularly “spicy” case is being tried the body
of the court is packed with briefless barristers
and solicitors and their boy-clerks, while the
gallery is filled with other unemployed. And the
stench! No wonder Darley, C. J., gets the corners
of his mouth well down and piles the adjectives on
the detective witnesses. If his looks do not belie
him, he would like to give everyone in court five
years.
* • *
The testimonial nuisance is outdone by
the banquet to the more or less distinguished
citizen going abroad. Full-length reports of the
affair get somehow into the paper's, and the
banqueted one is thus enabled to work the com-
mercial oracle in strange lands as a man of light
and leading.
Murdoch’s visit to Australia has particular
reference to the will of the late J. B. Watson,
under the terms of which Mrs. Murdoch’s children
are entitled to =£looo a year—the sum apportioned
to each family of Watsonian grandchildren, sub-
ject to the trustees’ control—for their collective
benefit now and hereafter. Seeing that there is
already a considerable accumulation of funds in
excess of the allowances made, on a sliding scale,
for the education of each family, the respective
pairs of parents are applying to the trustees “ to
expend the whole or a part of said accumulations
in either purchasing, or renting, likewise furnish-
ing, houses for each lot of children. The husband
of a rich man’s daughter has family cares of a
comfortable nature. How sweet to be able to stick-
up two bald-headed gentlemen in a lawyer’s office
and say, “Tommy, Billy, Maudy, &c., require a
new drawing-room suite. Kindly write a little
cheque out of their accumulations and oblige !”

Melbourne, once “the metropolis,” is
now the necropolis of Australia.
A N.S.W. medico lately proposed an
arrangement with his creditors. One of them—a
man of wealth, but socially unknown, proved
recalcitrant and threatened trouble. But a British
heart beat in his manly bosom, and he consented
at last to forego his claims in consideration of the
medico’s sassiety wife calling upon Mrs. Noovo
Reashe.
There are tragedies
that never get reported
in the press. Some
months ago a poor Melb.
woman, answering a loud
knock at her front door,
saw a strange man loung-
ing against the portal.
“Is your name Mrs.
Blank?” growled the
visitor. She answered to
that name. “Well, your
’usband was killed this
morning, over at So-and-
So’s,” said the news-
breaker, after pausing to
spit. Whereupon Mrs.
Blank, looking strange-
like, tottered into her
little back-room and
expired without further
comment, the double
funeral taking place next
day. It doesn’t take
much to upset some
women.

* * •
Dear Bulletin, —Last week a consignment
of beautifully fresh sea gar-fish was sold in Sydney
markets at prices ranging from 9d. to lod. per
basket. I happened to see the last week’s returns
of some Lake Macquarie fishermen. One man—
the most careful on the Lake—sent 16 baskets, for
which he received 15s. 6d. Three baskets sent by
another man realised Is. 6d. A third sent 10
baskets of mixed fish and exactly cleared expenses.
Yet, in Sydney, fish is so scarce and dear that the
cry is raised : “The fishing grounds are depleted,”
and Premier Dibbs actually suggests that the
harbours be closed for three years! If Parnell,
Edden, and other fishy champions would abolish
the middleman, or start a co-operative distributing
agency, they would do good ; the interminable
maunderings on side-issues turn the fishermen’s
stomachs sick. —Yours, Ponte.

N.S. Wales exported 2,880,0001b5. of
butter last year, the return from which was
■£114,000, or exactly per lb. As it was all of
prime quality, the whole of it would have been
consumed in Australia if sold at that price.
* * *
Melbourne masher collars are, in these
depressed days, made-up wrong side out—the
frayed face inside. This exposes the maker’s name
and other particulars, and the other day, on the
Block, an English johnnie proudly paraded,
oblivious of the fact that on his collar was con-
spicuously paraded the legend “ Made in Eng-
land.”

TO THE LADIES.
It i 3 generally admitted that you, ladies, have on
your shoulders the responsibility of the household,
and the care of providing for the welfare, at home,
of your husbands and the children.
You no doubt act up to the best of your abilities,
and yet there is one pertinent question which we
should like to ask you.
Do you give your children a drink
OUT OF THE TAP
when they are thirsty ?
If you do, you are running a fearful risk, especially
in the country, of bringing unwelcome visitors into
your home.

IN THE HOT WEATHER.
THIS IS
THE TIME
OF YEAR
WHEN
MEN AND WOMEN
BECOME
Weakened by the weather and run down gener-
ally. Few people understand why this is so. Let
us tell you why this is : The parts the weather
first affects are the kidneys; the urea is not
thrown off, but is forced back upon the lungs
through the blood, and disease results.
BUT IT WAS CAUSED
BY WEAKNESS OF THE KIDNEYS.
There is only one way known to medicine or men
for promptly checking kidney troubles and
restoring those great organs to health and strength,
and that is by the use of Warner’s Safe^Cubb.
It has stood the test of time.
It has saved tens of thousands of lives.
It has restored hundreds of thousands of
sufferers to good health.
It has made men stronger and healthier.
It has made women brighter and happier.
It stands alone in all these qualities.
Warner’s Safe Cube was a scientific discovery,
ranking in value to humanity with vaccination,
ether, chloroform and other great discoveries.
Thousands of alleged medicines live their little
day, and others, like the “blue glass’’ cure, Dr.
Brown Sequard’s Elixir of Life, and Pasteur’s
Cure for consumption, apparently aid for a time,
but “in the long run” fail to accomplish what
their ardent inventors hoped for. Not so, how-
ever, with Warner’s Safe Cube, which has stood
the test of time in curing desperate diseases, and
to-day is more widely known and is doing more
good than any one medicine in the world.

“ Persons of infamous character, pursuing
a dishonourable occupation !” Thus N.S.W. Judge
Windeyer about private detectives. Isn’t it about
time, then, that unofficial “shadowing” was
placed on an indictable par with “loitering,” and
that the divorce-case skunk came into unpleasant
contact with the “lawful visible” test so freely
applied to men whose only offence is impecu-
niosity ?

The Bulletin has been reading a story
entitled “Lost in the Australian Bush” in an

A week or two ago The Bulletin made
a few remarks about a mouldy who
calls round at the house of mourning, and tries to
trade off gloomy-looking memorial cards with
doggerel on them to the relatives of the deceased.
Whereupon, one firm in the trade, represented by
an eminently gentlemanly and carefully-dressed
potentate, sent along the following form of
apology, with a peremptory request that it should
appear in a conspicuous place :
In connection with a semi-humorous paragraph which
appeared in our issue of 3rd March, in reference to the
solicitation of orders for memorial cards, no allusion was
int' nded or implied to Blank and Cos., or their repreaenta-
tive, as this firm is of undoubted reputation, and are the
producers of first-class work in every branch of the print-
ra< e ‘, . , . ~ .
The senders of tins vigorous puff also enclosed a
sample card, with a gilt angel and three fowls
flying across it, and a verse in short metre at the
bottom, setting forth that Thomas —— is at rest,
It says a great deal for the deceased that he can
rest under such trying circumstances. The present
writer couldn’t. Anyhow, The Bulletin cheer-
fully accepts the assurance that the firm is of
“ undoubted reputation.”
Editor Bulletin, —Some of the
Queensland mourning -joard men send
round canvassers. Here in Melb. they
manage these things better. The daily
paper announces the death of John
Smith, aged 42, of Bungtown, and
straight away an envelope, apparently
holding a cabinet photo., is forwarded
to “The Executors of the late John
Smith, of Bungtown.” This on ex-
amination is found to contains highly-
finished eulogy of deceased with name,
age, and all particulars engraved. A
printed circular states the price and
asks that the card, if not approved
of, may be returned. The card has a
couple of verses of slop “poetry” on
it, but if you prefer your own poetry,
made to measure, which the slop poetry
isn’t, you can have it—so obliging is the
co. There are several of these engraving
corporations, and each takes particular
care to emphasise that it has “no con-
nection with the others.” —W.S.

A lady recently wrote to the
Melb. postmaster, asking for the ad-
dress of “a widower, rather short,
wears glasses, with one little boy two
years of aare,” whose name she had for-

to the little boy two years of age.

A letter from a seceding New Australian,
published in the S.A. Register, says the wives
amongst the 81 who have left the settlement would
not go back even if the men were inclined to.
Already the female pilgrim is harrowed with a
horrible dread that her hat is out of fashion.
Some men have luck. Especially a Melb.
man who, when he grossly assaulted a young lady>
was merely fined, though the public thought he
ought to have got three months. Now he gets
fined again for an offence which, were he nob well-
dressed, would earn him six months “straight”
—the alternative of said fine.

Mr. Huntington, in Liberator :
The camel has proved of material assistance in settling
the back country. About seven or eight years ago, a
string cf camels arrived on Upper Cooper’s Creek from
S.A., laden chiefly with flour. It was a perfect godsend,
as we had eaten no bread for ten weeks, and were living
entirely on beef. The drought did not break up for six
months after, and, but for the camels, w'e must have
existed on a diet of halt-starved beef for that period.
White men have been employed for years past on Mount
Murchison and other stations as drivers for the con-
veyance of rations and wool.

A Melbourne man, who has been support-
ing his father for several years past out of his
salary, has just discovered that the old man isn’t
his father at all. Considering how salaries go in
Melbourne just now the transaction is a pleasant
thing to look back upon. The real progenitor was
married in New York in ’6B, and shortly after
made a stratagio movement for Australia owing

eldest son, then an infant, grew up an (l
also came to Australia, and in Melbourne
his presumable parent called on him and
tearfully fell against him with his neg-
lected head upon the son’s bosom. He
answered all questions satisfactorily, and was
taken in and cared for. His billet as parent
lasted two years or so, but at the end of that
period he gave way to the yearning which comes
over almost everybody sooner or later to breathe
his tale into the ear of a friend, and thereby his
scheme was wrecked. The story was simply that
the real father was dead, and that, having got
sundry papers which once belonged to the corpse,
he had set up as a bogus parent on the strength of
them. This individual is now for sale, and anyone
who wants an affectionate father can have him
cheap.

# # *
Still another incident of the Maoriland
election. The wife of one candidate got up a com-
mittee of ladies to boom his interests, and the
henfranchised committee ate and drank much at
her house, and assumed airs of offensive patronage
to their hostess on the ground that they were
going to put her husband in. But it was under-
stood that they were all just fit to drop with
exhaustion through working in the good cause,
and the politician’s wife put up with them as best
she could—until the result was declared, and at
the local polling place that candidate had only
one vote. It was his wife’s own vote, too. The
committee was having refreshments at her expense
when the news arrived, and the vigour with
which it was thrown out into the street before it
had even time to wipe the crumbs off its mouth is
said to have been unprecedented.
A certain Yictorian hotel-keeper has ceased
to leave a bottle of beer outside his back door ere
retiring to rest. He used to put it there every
night for the policeman, and the policeman used
to tell him, next morning, how much he had
enjoyed the refreshment, but those times are gone
never to return. The landlord was always a heavv
sleeper. His wife, as far as he knew, slumbered
sweetly the while upon the adjoining pillow. A
thirsty traveller came along at 2 a.m. the other
night and rang violently until the landlord awoke
to the fact that his connubial partner was not
present. Not being a lonely man by disposi-
tion he went downstairs to find his missing
link. The first thing he found was a policeman s
outfit lying on the bar-counter. In a room hard
by he discovered a policeman au naturel the
same myrmidon whom he had been lubricating
gratis—in animated conversation with the lady of
the house. She was wearing a complete Venus
costume trimmed in the Godiva style, and the
landlord’s first impulse was to blush. Oneminute
later the thirsty traveller, waiting for the door to
be opened, was staggered by the apparition or
Adam and Eve flying from Eden. Adam s helmet
and other garments came flying after him, but Eve
left without encumbrances. Her subsequent
movements are known only to the pale moon and
a policeman, who, when last seen, was leaning
against a lamp post to put on his socks. The door
of the pub. closed noiselessly, and the rest is
silence.

The explosive energy exhibited in late
Presbyterian gatherings in N.S. W. would indicate
that that body is growing an Active Service and
an Anti-Humbug League within its own boun-
daries. Truly, all established forms of opinion
are being killed from within.

One clause from a bill which some old he-
female is now pushing through Massachusetts
(U.S.) State Legislature :
No woman shall appear at a public performance in a
dress or skirts which do not come within at least four
inches of the floor, and the waist of which is so constructed
that any part of her person is exposed below the neck ;
also no woman shall appear in tights or appear at public
performances in the high-kicking, serpentine, hip or skirt
dances.
There will be a splendid opening for the lawyer
who appears under this Act for a defendant,
charged with being unduly V-shaped in public,
as the bill apparently fails to define the exact spot
where a woman’s neck ends and her back begins.
The Bulletin, after considering many varieties
of evening dress, is unable to recognise the limit
to a woman’s neck.

A London cable tells of a Nihilist convent
in Russian Poland, the “nuns” of which were
found busily engaged in the manufacture of
explosive bombs. One “ nun ” shot two of the
military police. Three “ nuns” were killed in the
engagement and the remainder were arrested.
The genial Walter Sidney still pursues his
uninterrupted course of success at the Imperial
Hotel, Wynyard Square, Sydney. The constant
stream of travelling visitors who make this
hostelry their home, together with the crowds who
daily congregate after an enjoyable luncheon in
the tastefully-decorated smoking lounge, situated
between the dining-room and the main entrance-
hall, bear ample testimony to the fact that good
management and careful selection of the choicest
brands of refreshing drinks are the only certain
means to ensure prosperity in conducting an
establishment of this kind.

A WISH AND POSSIBILITIES.
/GENTLEMAN, 22, late Queensland, wishes to meef
quiet Girl, about 18. Liberal, c/o G.P.O.
—Sydney B. News advb.
Some maiden angel-fair, and graced
Of lithe lips tinged with hue of corals.
Swathed in a muslin dream of chaste
And fiercer fancies, interlaced,
Attenuated both in waist
And morals.
And if Fate with his wish agrees,
Let's hope—wbate’er occurs—
That it he only gets her, he’s
Prepared with gladsome jubilees
To thank the gods upon his knees,
Or hers.
D.W.
Dear Bulletin,—l am a layman mean,
My head isn’t made of wood,
So I rise, in the name of his Gracious Queen,
To sit upon Justice Hood —
To sit upon him in the layman sense
Of a figure of speech that’s low.
I may be dull, or extremely dense,
But, sticking to things I know,
I’ll roughly strike my heart strings, whence
Rude doggerel strains will flow.
It isn’t a question of lawyers’ law.
Or a matter of lawyers’ righ t
That moves me to flourish an inky paw
In the face of a legal light.
’Tis little I care about musty Acts
Or humbugging “rules to quash
As a simple supporter of layman facts
I svvear that the Judge talks hosh.
“ Yer Honor,” I say, as his brow contracts,
“ Your libel on us won’t wash.”
That yarn won’t wash, in the layman sense
Of a metaphor impolite.
A lawyer’s “ fault ” is a large offence
Wherever a wrong’s not right;
A damned offence, in our layman eyes —
We give it a nasty name.
Woe to the clerkling rash who tries
A similar kind of game ! .
I wear no wig, but “ there ain’t no flies
On my argument, ail the same.
Dear Bulletin,—l am a layman mean,
And probably not much good,
But I rise, in the name of his Gracious Queen,
To differ from Justice Hood.
The lawyer fox in his dingy den
Is sinfully slow to “ part; ”
Yet, out in the world, live struggling men
Who spit at the deeds called smart.
If honour comes not within legal ken,
Still it lurks in the layman heart.
E.F.

Professor Thulinius.—My Dear Sir,—l feel I should be want-
ing in duty, did I neglect the opportunity of sending you my
testimony as to thp wondrous efficacy of your rheumatic
specific. For the past ten years I have been a complete
martyr to rheumatism in all its forms. I have tried doctors
. . - • . with a view of effecting a cure for this most trying
disease I am well-known, and scores can testify
that at best I could only crawl about with difficulty. . . “
After leaving Little Bay Hospital at the close of last year I
was persuaded to try a bottle of your “Thulinius,” and in a
week or so afterwards I could get about fairly well. Upon
again suffering a relapse last February, I took another bottle,
with an equally successful result; but at last I determined to
go through a course of treatment, and I have just completed
my fourth bottle, with the gratifying knowledge that I am
quite free from pain, can walk any reasonable distance with-
out fatigue, have an excellent appetite, and feel myself again
competent to re-enter my profession, added to which my oft-
repeated attacks of pleurisy have become a thing of the past.
I have not dieted myself in any way, so all the credit is due to
your wonderful remedy, under Providence, that my blood is
becoming purer daily, and its effect upon my liver and kid-
neys most pronounced. ... I shall only be too glad
personally to convince fellow-sufferers of the benefits to be
derived from a course of your treatment, as exemplified in
my own case, after ten years’ martyrdom.—l remain, yours
faithfully, W. H. NEWTON NEWMAN (Teacher of Elocution
and twenty-five years Actor and Manager).
[The Rev. J. D. Langley, St. Philip’s Parsonage, and Mr. H. H.
Lusk, Barrister-at-Law, can testify as to the crippled state of
Mr. Newman prior to his taking “Thulinius.”]

On the Norddeutscher Lloj'd steamers the punkahs are
worked by the stewards. They stand outside and pull tht
ropes through the portholes. We have it terribly hot at
present, and it is pitiful to see these poor wretches grafe
away at the ropes to keep us cool. With a little expense
the engines could do this work.
It has now been clearly proved that the
N.S.W, Pastoralists’ Union had moved to reduce
shearing-rates for the coming season before ever
tne Shearers’ Union Conference sat and con-
sidered the question of refusing to furnish P.U.
references to squatters. P.U. members were
circularised in January to the effect that the
P.U. executive committee was considering the
question of altering the shearing agreement; the
Shearers’ Conference did not meet till February 2.
The P.U. now declines to recognise the ’9l agree-
ment and demands from each shearer a reference
and —&l deposit !
London newspaper “ad.” :
JDERSONS in Spiritual Difficulty and Trouble may
see by appointment, matured Christian ladies
and gentlemen (in camera and confidence), at 17
Beaumont-st., Marylebone (5 minutes’ walk from
Baker-st. Station). There need be no reference to
Church or creed, and certainly will not be any attempt
to obtain money or to proselytise.
Everything possible will be done to lead such in-
quirers to the living Christ.
Those who are sick and need spiritual help will be
visited by arrangement.
All letters to be addressed the Rev. Darlow Sarjeant,
Nicholas House, 17 Beaumont-street, Marylebone, W.
(marked “ Chambers.”)
Underneath this notice, as a horrible example of
the general unfitness of things, appears an advt.
headed “Vermin Trap.”
Retrenchment bears fruit in the Far
North of S. A. There is a small coastal town
where one constable is both lockup-keeper and
sergeant-in-charge. He gets a shilling a day
to tucker each prisoner, and he makes it pay better
than Mt. Morgan. Recently 18 Javanese were in
for 14 days, also a white man for eight, through
“ doing up his screw ”in snake-juice. The latter
was put into a small cell with 13 Javs. for com-
panions. They never had a wash —so soap was
saved. “Tucker” consisted of sweet potatoes
twice a day, bread and salt junk once every third
day. No tea—only a bucket of water. Sweet
potatoes are 3s. per cwt., and salt beef 12s. 6d.
per cwt. The lockup-keeper thus netted 15s. per
day out of his batch of prisoners. No wonder he
hurries up his “min” when he bears of Javs.

“Self-defence was the object aimed at when arming
the Sydney police with revolvers. If the weapon is other-
wise employed, constables will become particularly
citizens, and cause more alarm to the public than to the
burglar and garrotter.”
