SPORTING NOTIONS.
Crozier, the wealthy S.A. sport, owner of
Vakeel, Fulham, &c., sells his stud prior to giving up
racing. Vakeel won last Adelaide Cup.
♦ • • *
The sentence of three months’ gaol passed upon
“ Gala,” for sweep-promoting at Sydney, has been altered
to a fine of £5O and sureties for good behaviour. The
difference which has been made by the law between the
N.S.W. sweepmakers constitutes a gross scandal, and the
public, as is usual where obvious favouritism is officially
shown, are not slow to attribute definite motives and
point their fingers at those apparently concerned in the
job.
* • * •
The apparent moral of recent Vie. race-meetings
is— “Buy S.A.-bred stock.” The latter have won all along
the line.
* • * *
E. Jones, whose fast bowling won the cricket-
match for S.A. against Victoria, received as a reward £2O,
a pair of boots, cricket bat, the historical ball mounted in
silver, and a silk umbrella for “ the missus.” Premier
Kingston donated the boots.
» * • »
A good cricket-match in Adelaide. The Aus-
trals (the new junior team) made 423 against Norwood.
The tatter, without the Giffens, 409. Veteran Jimmy
Gooden, who played in interprovinoial matches 15 years
ago, scored 137.
• * * *
Tom Sullivan, of Maoriland (says The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent), had a great gathering the
night he opened his Hammersmith pub., to wish him
success. At all events, he has started with the kudos
(whatever that may be worth) attaching to him as the
trainer of Harding (who beat Bubear), and to the belief
that by-and-bye, if he does not drink too much of his own
“swankey,” he will be able to take down Stanbury’s
number on the Thames.
* » » »
Hugh Callow, billiard - marker, White Hart
Hotel, Haydonton, N.S.W., last week made a break of
1115, mostly from cannons.
» » * *
Tommy Corrigan urges that steeplechases should
be run earlier in the afternoon—as “ the farther the sun is
from its ztnith the more it glints on to the fences, which
consequently cannot be seen by horses.”
• * * *
As he is matched against Sullivan, the Maori-
lander now settled in England, Lambert, of N.S.W., will
soon have a chance of proving whether he is as good as
his friends claim— i.e., better than any living sculler.
* * • *
The American (?) champion, Corbett, was born
in Tuam, Ireland. The English champion, Mitchell, cer-
tainly hails from Brummagem, but his parents were
Irish and so is his temper, and very bad at that. Which
recalls the Aeous’ praise of Peter Jackson’s “Anglo-
Saxon pluck." Corbett and Jackson, it is fixed, are to
fight in London.
• • • •
Geo. Giffen heads the Australian batting
average with 75, M‘Leod coming next with 43, Moses
third 42.4.
* • * »
To account for Titan’s recovery and reversal
of form there are several queer stories. One is that the
gelding, as he got old, used to hang back on hearing
other horses behind him. To cure this habit his ear-
drums were pierced, with the result that he again began
to forge ahead. A more probable yarn is that the horse
was “got at” soon after being purchased by Mr. Brodribb,
and did not recover for a long time from the dose
administered
♦ * • •
Wendouree (Ballarat) crew easily won the Yic.
Champion Eight contest last Saturday, three lengths
ahead of the hitherto unbeaten Yarra Yarra, who were
in turn five past the much-boomed Mercantiles. Corio
Bay (Geelong), the other starter, threw up the sponge at
the end of two miles, and rowed silently home. The
result will do much to liven up the sport down South, for
the championship has, for the last three years, been practi-
sally a walk-over for the now waning Yarras. The time
was 17min. 6sec., as against the 15min. 45sec.of 12 months
ago over the same course of 3 miles 330yds. Why don’t
the rowing authorities organise an interprovincial club
race ? If, say, the Wendouree, Warrnambool, Yarra Yarra,
North Shore, East Sydney and Mercantile (N.S.W.) could
arrange a meeting, it would cause ten times the interest,
among oarsmen, of the ordinary interprovincial affair.
* • ♦ »
So great is the horror of Yic. Football Associa-
tion at the idea, however remote, of professionalism in
connection with their strictly amateur game, that that
virtuous body decided to abolish the payment of the gate-
keepers employed at matches by the day, and to sub-
stitute a sliding-scale in proportion to the attendance at
each match. For instance, on a wet day, when the
takings fail to reach £2O, the sodden janitor has Is. 6d.
docked off his fair-weather screw, and so on. For petty
meanness the V.F. A. about takes the cake, and its action
is entitled to take its place among the doings of the
champion mean men. So writes a correspondent.
« ♦ * *
The Irex won the Intercolonial Sailing Race at
Brisbane, with Cansebie second, and Portia third. The
Bulletin was actually second last. If this happens again
the name must be changed.
• » * *
At Manly (Sydney), last Saturday, Arthur
Cavill, brother of Ernie of that ilk, won the 200 yards
amateur championship of N.S.W. in a style joyful to
behold. He is the coming swimmer, and will probably
stand in the front rank of aquatic cracks in the near
future. Apropos, on the same day, at the North Shore,
one of the Misses Cavill won a ladies’ race. Verily they
are a great family of swimmers.
Re Portsea’s record for the Champion, sm.
234sjc. : O i beiting Chester in '7B, First King won in a
canter in sm. 26sec. James Wilson says that if pushed
the horse could have got home in 5.20. “ Augur ” Chap-
man, even when the doings of Carbine and Abercorn
were' fresh in the public mind, declared in print that First
King, when he won his first Champion—the only occasion
on which he was fully trained-was the best horse ever
seen in Australia.
* * * ♦
The syndicate of which Mr. J. B. Clark is the
figurehead is understood to consist of Mr J. C. Bowden
(of Cramsie, Bowden and 00., well-known in connection
with the Murray River steamboat trade), W. Bailey and
Martin Loughliu (both of Ballarat), T. Payten, and T.
Hales.
• ♦ * *
The world’s tallest cricketer is said to be
“Jumbo” Reid, a young Cornstalk playing in Scotland
with the Edinburgh Australians. He stands 6ft. BJin. in
his socks and fields at point.
• * • *
Ballarat went into deep mourning on the day of
Paddy M'Gowan’s funeral. Shops shut up, flags were
lowered to half-mast, boys refrained from playing
marbles, and the funeral of the deceased jockey was a mile
in length. To attain true greatness in Australia a man
must go to scale at seven stone, or be able to lick Peter
Jackson. The average-sized person has simply no chance
of glory.
6 '• • * *
A verse from “ The Death of Poor Paddy
M'Gowan,” a poem published at a penny:—
It’s a terrible tale to tell,
Of an accident that befell
In the Bourke Handicap, that awful mishap
O’er all the course cast a spell.
The horses were ranged up in a line,
Like satin their smooth coats did shine;
The start it was made, no time was delayed.
For victory each rider did pine.
Poor Paddy M'Gowan with victory flushed,
And hope in his bosom but borne to be crushed ;
For just as the horses were rounding the bend
’Twas Linburn who stumbled and caused his sad end.
* ♦ » *
The young jockey should contrast the careers of
Hales and Gorry and draw his own conclusion.
* * * •
Mr. D. H. Harvey, for many years the right-
hand man of “Tattersall" Adams, has retired from his
position owing to ill-health, and proposes to take a long
rest.- To all intents and purposes Mr. Harvey was, at
least, half of “Tattersall,” George Adams being the
other half, and he had much to do with making the
famous consultations the most popular, most honest, and
most successful gamble in Australia. The retiring
manager got a stupendous send-off from his staff, the
said send-off including a silver-fitted travelling-bag, an
illuminated address, and the customary refreshments
which are the Australian substitute for the finer
emotions.
• * ♦ •
The N.S.W. Interprovincial Eight-oar people
are having their annual little snarls. Messrs. Robson and
Quist are the new coaches—vied Johnson, resigned in a
huff.
• • • •
Some good trotting times should be put up at
Sydney Agricultural Show at Easter. The track is the
only one about Sydney fit to make fast time on, and there
will be the novelty of seeing the whole field “on the job.
••. • •
Dear Bulletin, —Your contributor errs ini stat-
ing that there were cleverer men than Jem Mace with the
gloves who would not dare to lace him in the ring, and
that it was to his wrestling abilities he owed his victories
over big men like King ; also, that modern glove-fights
demand less than half the skill and courage of the old
P.R. As a matter of fact, Jem Mace was the only scien-
tific boxer of his day, and it is doubtful if the latter days
have produced his peer with the mittens. A small man
had no more show with a good big man in the bare-
knuckle days than he has in the present with the gloves,
and Mace and Sayers were the only two middle-weights
who held their ends up against all comers, and that
simply because there were no good big men to meet
them. Mace beat King, but King returned the compli-
ment at their second meeting ; and, as for Sayers, he met
Heenan, not a fighter, but a wrestler, and when the
British crowd, seeing their man to all intents and pur-
poses beaten, rushed the ring-as they smashed Black
Molineux’s fingers when he was beating their champion,
Tom Cribb—Heenan jumped the ropes and ran like a
deer, a hundred yards or so, to show how fresh he was.
As for courage, the raw meat little , un could hit a big
’un drop to avoid punishment, and take his “ time,
but’ the latter-day gladiator must not go down with-
out a blow, and if knocked down he must be up
in ten seconds, without the assistance of his seconds, and
battle on for the remainder of the three-minute round.
Little “Bendigo” beat the colossal Ben Gaunt without
showing any wrestling ability or courage. Bendy would
lump in, hit the giant and fall down on his knees, which
so savaged Gaunt that he “ wiped ” Bendigo across the
mouth whilst the little ’un was on his knees, and the fight
was given to Bendigo on the foul. Had your contributor
seen the boxing contest between Smith and M'Carty, now
both in the States, at Larry Foley’s White Horse Hotel,
privately, one afternoon, he would for ever hold his peace
as to the dogged courage and ability to take punishment
required in a Queensberry contest fought to the bitter
end.-W.
Linotype type-setting machines are being
introduced by a syndicate of Australian news-
papers on behalf of which Manager Wynne, of
Sydney D.T., lately visited America. In the
D.T. office aforesaid there are now 74 compositors,
52 of whom are at once to be displaced by the
machines, and so, presumably, it will be with the
other daily papers. A shocking prospect for the
poor printer during the coming winter.
Like master, like servant. Two Chartered
00. troopers are accused of having indirectly
caused the slaughter of Wilson’s party in Mata-
beleland, by suppressing an offer from Lobengula
to surrender, and stealing <£looo which he sent
with the offer. How fit that dog should eat dog,
that robbers should rob robbers, that the blood of
Maxim-murdered Matabele should be avenged on
the shedders of blood at the hands of their own
comrades!
Around the non-publication of a certain
Australian military commander’s report there is
to be woven a pretty little romance. The com-
mandant is noted forhis plain-speaking and truth-
fulness. The report in question fairly bristles
with truths. One of these “truths” seriously
affected an officer high in the service—an officer
with a very pretty and charming wife. She had
very many friends, but none more steadfast than
the Premier. “ And that was how Bill Adams
won the Battle of Waterloo.” That is one version
of the affair.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these

Editor Bulletin, —If the Governments of
Victoria and Tasmania want to clear the snakes
from the islands in Bass Straits, just let them turn
loose a few dozen pigs there. In such confined
areas the swine will soon do the work. —M.G.
A well-known parson is bringing his little
son up in the way which he should go, and
mamma most assiduously reads the Bible to the
little chap every day. Result —the enfant terrible
came to her the other day and said : “ Oh,
mummy, feel my side, I’m sure I’m going to have
a wife.”
The aboriginal rough-rider of Strathmore
Station, Q., lately mounted a filly renowned as a
bucker. Davey put “the M'Guiness” on her,
and at the first buck he was literally shot out of
his boots, they being left in the stirrup-irons and
Davey in a China apple-tree. Next.
“ The majority of prisoners are convicted
for what are practically venial offences. For
instance, in Vic., in 1891, there were men and
women imprisoned for having committed, in the
aggregate, 11,000 offences, of which 8000 com-
prised—being idle and disorderly, drunkenness,
insulting behaviour, petty larceny, obscene
language, and vagrancy : all sins of the weak,
foolish, and unfortunate.” — Age.
The Bulletin’s last Xmas number, which
consisted mainly of bones and ghosts, seems to
have been really cheerful reading compared to
some English publications at the same festive
season. One joyous tale in a British periodical
concerned a marriage:—“A young man, on the
eve of his marriage, discovers that his father died
o I a terrible disease in its most malignant form,
and that he had the poison in his veins. He tells
his betrothed the ghastly secret at a Christmas
party, and she then confides to him that her
father is a prisoner in Broadmoor, behaving mur-
dered her mother during an attack of hereditary
insanity, and that two of her sisters murdered
their infants and then committed suicide, and
that sort of thing runs in the family. Under those
circumstances they agree to get married at once,
and on the bridal night they both take poison,
and are found dead in the morning side by side.”
Thus a Queensland correspondent:—
Dear Bulletin, —Are there any means of forcing a
contractor for public works to account for the so-called
medical fund, which is a sum of money deducted by the
contractor from the employes wages, with their know-
ledge, but without their consent? So far, the practice is
to deduct from each any sura the contractor
thinks fit, not exceeding Is. per week, or portion of a
week. With this he provides indifferent medical attend-
ance, in all serious cases sending the sick or injured to
the local hospital upon a subscriber’s ticket, where they
are promptly labelled “Pauper.” The balance of the
fund the contractor deals with as he pleases. The
amount collected yearly has been, and still is, very large ;
on some contracts where the deduction is a shilling
a week per the total runs into thousands.
On one Queensland railway the deduction amounted to
£3O per week, or a round total of not less than £5OOO at
the completion of the contract. This contractor took out
a policy with an assurance co. to protect himself against
his employes in the event of any claims being made good
against him for negligence or carelessness in carrying out
the works. And the premium on that policy is supposed
to have been paid out of the so-called Medical Fund. I
do not know if the same system prevails in the other pro-
vinces, but if so the total amount filched from the
labourers must be very large indeed. Surely the Govt,
which inserts a special clause in the contract empowering
the contractor to deduct this money, should also provide
for a public audit of the account at the completion of the
contract and prior to the final payments being made to
the contractor.— Free Lance.
Dear Bulletin, —The chief fault found by
N.S.W. bush State-school teachers with recent
retrenchment is its unequal incidence. The
teacher in the backblocks has been docked more
in proportion to his difficulties, expenses, and dis-
comforts than the more favoured ones near towns.
Most schools in the bush are provisional, half-
time, or house to-house. Before Ist July last, the
salaries for provisional teachers were—lst class,
.£96 ; 2nd class, <£B4; 3rd class, <£72. These were
reduced to Ist class, <£9l; 2nd class, <£80; 3rd
class -£6B. The old rate for house-to-house schools
was £5 per child on the average attendance, up
to a maximum of -£lOO per annum. Now the rate
is .£4 15s. on the average attendance up to a
maximum of £95. Half-time schools were paid,
Bth class, .£156; 9th class, <£l32; 10th class,
<£loB per annum; and now, Bth class,
.£136; 9th class, .£113; 10th class, ,£9l.
In provisional and house-to-house schools the
salaries vary according to the average attendance.
Half-time schools are liable to reduction if the
average falls below a specified number for a quar-
ter. Is it not unfair that a bush teacher, with a
salary of <£ls6, should have =£2o knocked off his
screw, while a teacher in charge of a 6th class
school in a town, getting a salary of .£216, gets
docked but .£ll, and a teacher holding the same
classification in a cosy locality is only retrenched
<£9 in .£180? The only complaint I have heard
from teachers is that the Government did not deal
fairly all round, and that the weaker portion of
the service came in for the severest retrenchment,
which undoubtedly it did.—Yours, A Teacher.

Greville’s “Year-Book of Australia,” for
1894, is better than its predecessors, if possible;
abridged, but concentrated, and remaining the
most compendious Australian book of __ reference.
Special articles deal with agriculture, dairy-farm-
ing, wine and fruit-growing, meat-supply and the
wool-trade; and there is the usual review of the
year’s work in trade, politics and art.

ocrats. They hail with delight the new lease of
power it has given them. Thus do Anarchists
play into the hands of humanity’s oppressors. If
not the allies, bomb-throwers are the tools of
plutocracy. Eorce begets force, whether from
above or below. Eorce may have its mission, but
not until a majority has expressed its mandate
at the polls, and that mandate has been overridden
by force. Then revolution, not retail murder, is
in order. —Ex.
Dear Bulletin, Under the signature
“X.L.,” I wrote'a paragraph in your columns
about a statement by Bishop Webber, of Queens-
land, and it is clerically stigmatised as an anony-
mous lie. The date was made somewhat too
recent, which does not affect the essence. Ex-
plicitly, then, I read a very lengthy letter, in the
London Guardian, signed with the name of this
Bishop, urging young English clergymen to visit
his diocese, for periods of five years. He showed
that this need not interfere with their preferment
in England, and further that it would not be
requisite for them to be intimately associated with
the Queensland people, in any way. Under the
title of “Brisbane Diocese,” I published a brief
article in Melbourne Imperial, to the same effect
as my “X.L.” paragraph.—Yours, Herbert
Palmer.
A little while back there was held in Mel-
bourne a sale of “ Art Furniture ” which had once
belonged to a prominent land-boomer. He had
settled it upon his wife, and the sale was held_ in
her name. The very auctioneer’s catalogue which
is before us as we write, must have cost as much
to print as would produce a small volume of
poems. Items from the list of articles which used
to adorn the “Reception Room Cabinet
with elaborate carvings, mirrors, alcoves” (the
catalogue describes this piece of furniture as being
exceptionally beautiful and artistic), “magnificent
upright grand piano, by Schwechten —double
oblique trichord, overstrung,” &c., &c., “ three
suites window drapings, in finest silk tapestry,
bordered with olive plush, very beautiful and
costly ” (Damn the expense !), “ costly Majolica
urn on pedestal,” “magnificent real Satsuma
Japanese vase,” “ pair very fine Japanese
bronze vases,” another “ magnificent real
Satsuma vase,” a “pair very large Satsuma
vases,” a “ suite of Majolica vases,” “ magnificent
real Dresden china ewer,” “magnificent console
glass, with ottoman, in finest olive plush,” “ pair
Japanese china vases,” “2 blue Venetian vases,”
“costly real Satsuma Japanese vase;” once
more, “large Majolica vase,” “ large Japanese
bronze coin,” “ pair finest bronze bas-reliefs —very
costly” (hang the cost!), “magnificent cabinet
organ,” “extra-finest quality Persian carpet.”
The dining-room contained more vases —all of
them costly—and extra-finest quality expensive
furniture, odd chunks of verlu, original oil-paint-
ings by German artists described severally as of
great breadth and power, small but choice,
one of the finest examples of this great
artist, another grand example of the great-
est of German marine painters, &c. More
vases, more carved cabinets, more splendid ebon
and gold upright pianofortes, more Persian
carpets, Brussels carpets, Axminster carpets, and
promiscuous parvenu magnificence in the other
rooms. Also, in the yard, a phaeton, pair of
ponies, and hack —in all, 523 lots. There was
scarcely one article of local production in the cata-
logue. Everything was German except the
ponies, a cow, some roosters and an Aylesbury
drake.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being. effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

between Longreach and station is so bare
and destitute of vegetation that you could chase a
flea the whole distance and never lose sight of
him.”

(In Liquidation.)
Tenders are Invited
tmd will be received up to 12 noon, SATURDAY,
7th APRIL, for the purchase of the whole of this
Company’s property situate at Montalbion (N.Q.).
The property offered is as follows
Smelting Machinery,
1 60-ton capacity water-jacket furnace; 1 30-ton
ditto; 1 15-ton ditto; 1 tram-elevator; platform
scales ; “ Hope ” stonebreaker, 15" x 7"; 1 30-h.p.
Robey engine; 3 roasting furnaces; 3 baker’s
blowers; 1 Manchester pump; 1 Tangye do ; 1
Blake do (together throwing about 20,000 gals,
per hour); 3000 ft. assorted W.I. piping; tanks of
6000 gals, capacity; 2 Multitubular boilers,
14' x s'; and all appliances complete and in
thorough order.
Concentrating Machinery.
2 30ft. Linkenbach patent Buddies with 2 Spitz-
gastens, &c M 6 Jordan and Cummins patent
jiggers, 2 sets Cornish rolls, 3 elevators, 1 Blake
and Marsdens’ stonebreaker, 7 revolving screens,
sampling tables, &c., &c.
General Machinery,
Spare shafting, piping, belting, firebars, fire-
doors, steel rails (about 3 miles), trucks, bullion
moulds, slag pots, slag trucks, 60ft. architnedean
screw conveyor, Buliivant’s wire tramway com-
plete for 1500 fathoms, spare pulleys, Briquette
machine, cupel furnaces and frames, 2 Avery’s
6 ton weighbridges, 1 Ruston and Proctor’s 12h.p.
portable engine, 6in. centrifugal pump, 2£in.
rotary pump, 1 Bh.p. loco, boiler, donkey feed
pump, Bh.p. winding engine, lOh.p. winding
engine, lOh.p. multi tubular boiler, Blake feed
pump, and 20 tons charcoal and a quantity of
lime and iron and firewood.
Buildings.
Office and dwelling two storeys, 36' x 30';
Kitchen, toolhouse, cartshed, stables, store, and
weighbridge office. Dwelling and office furniture;
smelting shed, 210'x 110'; concentrating shed,
172 ft. x 60ft.; laboratory and shed, 93ft. x 50ft.
Bathrooms, blacksmith’s shops, &c. Engine,
mine, and blacksmiths’ sheds erected at all the
mines.
The movable tools are estimated at, at least,
£1000; the stores at £lOOO.
There are 1500 tons of payable ore on the sur-
face, and 2000 tons of matte estimated to contain
40 oz. silver per ton.
The mines are exempt from work (or let on
tribute) until 26th April next, and all workings
are in thorough order.
The assaying and analytical plant is complete in
every requisite and material.
This is the most complete plant for smelting and
concentrating silver ores that has ever been placed
on the Australian markets. The machinery is all
good, and the purchaser may commence operations
without a week’s delay. Fullest enquiries as to
probable success are solicited.
An unfortunate entanglement with the banking
institutions during the late financial crisis, at
which time the capital of the Company had been
called up, forced this Company into liquidation,
the outcome of which is that the property must be
realised upon for the benefit of the creditors.

The latest big N.S.W. cave is named “the
Slattery cave ” —after the local Minister of Jus-
tice. As might be surmised, it is indeed a colossal
vacancy.
Pall Mall Gazette has been inviting
contributors to send what they consider “ the most
pathetic few lines.” The article grows in Australia
sometimes :
Melbourne, Monday. — Ernest Knox, hanged this
morning, said “ Good bye ” to his mother at 9 o’clock last
night. She is old and grey-haired, and was allowed to
kiss her son through the iron-grating.
The Bulletin acknowledges receipt of
No. 2 of the Australasian edition of the Photo-
grapio Review of Reviews, owned and pub-
lished by Baker and Rouse, the well-known photo-
graphic supply merchants. Mr. Edwin J. Welch
is editor, and has done his duty remarkably well,
the local notes and articles being full of interest.
The photo-engravings are splendidly turned out
by the Electric Photo. Co. The Review is alto-
gether an admirable sixpenn’orth.
Two handsome trophies presented at the
Manly Swimming Club’s carnival last Saturday
were the work of Messrs. Stewart Dawson and
Co., the well-known jewellers, of George-street,
Sydney. One was the 200-yards’ championship
trophy, and consisted of a solid silver shell
mounted on a plush easel, with silver mermaids
represented as swimming underneath. The other
—which was presented by Stewart Dawson and
Co. —consisted of a beautifully-finished staghorn
inkstand. Both were perfect in workmanship, of
the most tasteful design, and in every way worthy
of the firm’s high reputation.

Herald :
COOK.—Charles Thomas Haine Cook, Brighton, 1810,
son of Colonel Jones, Equerry to George 111. ; a proteg6
of tha Princess Sophia ; his mother was Maid of Honour,
friend, companion, to Queen Caroline; nephew of the
late Countess of Essex. His brother is mentioned in “Men
of the Times ” —superintendent of schools of England,
Ireland, Scotland. Entered His Majesty’s navy, served
5 years’ active service on the coast of Africa, came as first
mate to this colony three times with the late Captain
Geary, B.N. Father of Mrs. John Ferguson, of Castle-
reagh-street.
A dandily got-up volume, just published
by Geo. Robertson & Co., is “ Ironbark Chips and
Stockwhip Cracks,” by G. Herbert Gibson,
(“Ironbark.”). The intents of the book do not
pretend to harmonise with the bushy expressiveness
of the title ; out of 32 items, only 13 exhale any
characteristically Australian emanations. Where-
ever “Ironbark” does swing off, with naturalness,
the free, coarse exaggeration of bush “ pitch,” his
matter goes swimmingly; and, as typical of a
class, has the poetical merits due to appreciative
observation. Bulletin readers will come across
one or two old friends in fragments that were first
printed in this paper. The book is very pictur-
esquely and humorously illustrated by Percy F. S.
Spence and Alf. Vincent. As a rough-slab hut of
pioneer poesy (so to speak) “ IronbarkChips”should
sell well among Australians.
*
It is now just four years since the Smith
Premier typewriter was put on the market, and
already there are 35,000 instruments in use. This
is about the best possible proof of its excellence.
It is durable, simple of construction, rapid in its
movement, produces first-class work, and is pos-
sessed of many other advantages. It can do
almost everything, in fact, except drink, and
when this defect is overcome, and it is a genial
companion as well as a typewriter, it will be about
perfect. Fisher and Lingham, of 3 Victoria
Arcade, Sydney, are local agents.
Ernest Knox, the burglar who was inter-
rupted while ransacking a Williamstown pawn-
shop and who shot the pawnbroker’s son in the
scrimmage, was duly hanged at Melbourne last
Monday. His case was hopeless from the first.
The Patterson Government, a few days ago,
reprieved the brute who shot a man for trying to
prevent him beating his mother, and even reduced
the customary life sentence to 15 years; but for
the criminal who shoots a man for interrupting
him when he is stealing Property there is no
shadow of hope whatever. Property, in the
Pattersonian code, is a lot more important than
anybody’s mother. Knox, by the way, committed
Sacrilege as well as murder. The pawnshop is the
temple of Property, and Knox slew the son of the
high priest.
The facts of that quarrel between N.S.W.
Premier Dibbs and Commandant Hutton seem
now clear enough. The latter seems inclined to
set up Gov. Duff as a sort of rival to the Premier.
Now, His Ex. is titular Commander-in-Chief. He
is also “Queen Victoria’s ” representative. George
of Cambridge, who is Oornmander-in-Chief in Eng-
land, is merely the permanent head of a branch
of the War Office, and Queen Victoria would not
allow him to make direct official communications
to her even if he were fool enough to attempt it.
If she did so she would soon be taught better.
Now, this is precisely what Gov. Duff has allowed
Commandant Hutton (the real N.S.W. Com-
mander-in-Chief) to do; and the Governor, who has
had years of experience in the Commons, and has
also been in office, ought to know better. He knows
that Admiral Smith, who commands the British
squadron in these seas, sends all his official reports
to the Admiralty direct, and would refuse under
any circumstances to acknowledge him as his
superior officer. As for Commandant Hutton,
who has never held a command before, he can
only be excused for his folly in sending his report
direct to the Governor on the ground that he is
utterly ignorant of the conditions on which he
holds his position as a servant of the people of
N.S.W., though that excuse can hardly be valid
when it is remembered that he has held staff
appointments which must have made him ac-
quainted with the usual official practice.
Wheat is selling at one spot in the Mallee
(Vie.) at a maximum of Is. 9d. a bushel.
Mr, George Colquhoun (of Allen and Allen)
has been appointed N.S.W. Crown Solicitor. The
choice is an excellent one. A first-class lawyer,
and an honest, determined and methodical man
are combined in Mr. Colquhoun.
The ceremony of setting the keystone of
the magnificent Sydney building of the Mutual
Life Insurance Company of New York was per-
formed on March 14 by the N.S.W. Premier in
presence of a large crowd of spectators. The new
structure stands at the corner of Pitt-street and
Martin-place, directly opposite the General Post
Office, and will cost which is a pretty
solid guarantee of the Company’s good faith.
The Mutual Life claims to be the foremost life
insurance company in the world. Its assets at
the end of 1893 were its annual in-
come was .£8,500,000, and the surplus of assets
available for bonus distribution, after fully pro-
viding for liabilities, was £3,675,000. And the
whole of this business has been built up in the last
fifty years ! At the conclusion of the ceremony
a large number of the most notable men in
Sydney sat down to a luncheon given by Major
Z. O. Rennie, the Company’s Australian represen-
tative, in honour of the event. The toasts of
“The Queen,’’ “The President of the United
States,” “The Parliament of New South Wales,”
“The Mutual Life Insurance Company of New
York,” “The Architect and Contractors,” “Our
American Kindred,’’and “The Chairman” (Major
Rennie), were amongst those honoured. Those
present included no less than four Ministers of the
Crown (the Premier, the Colonial Treasurer, the
Minister for Lands, and the Minister for Public
Instruction), the Speaker of the Legislative As-
sembly, the Mayor of Sydney, Messrs. Pilcher,
Pigott, and Greville, Ms.L.C., Mr. M'Millan,
M.P., Colonel Bell, U.S. Consul, and many
other prominent men. The speakers congratu-
lated Major Rennie on the enterprise shown by
his company in erecting such a building, and
wished that the business done in Australia might
justify and reward the confidence shown in our
future. Major Rennie, in returning thanks for
this expression of good opinion, said it had been
his privilege to experience nothing but goodwill
since he set foot in Australia 'seven years ago.
He wished Australians to understand that when
they are offered a contract with the Mutual Life
of New York, they are offered a partnership in
the wealthiest, the largest, and most honourable
association of individuals that exists in this world
—a contract guaranteed by a company that has
progressed and prospered continuously for half a
century under every financial and social condition,
and whose' reputation for honesty and fair dealing
has never been questioned. (Cheers.)

TUESDAY,
According to Captain Cook’s journal, we
glean that the Maoris called Australia “Ulima?
roa.” It has been suggested that Australia was
originally the barren central plateau of an immense
and fertile continent covering a great part of what
is now the Indian and South Pacific oceans.
Dear Bulletin, —In your recent issue you
spoke of grubs, 4ft. long, in Gippslahd. There
seems to be something wrong there* , Probably the
beast really meant is the big Gippsland earth*
worm (Megascolides Australis) described by Prof,
Baldwin Spencer, of Melb. University. I have
handled, alive, and dissected, dead, specimens of
this worm over six feet long. When dead the
worm emits a hideous odour resembling creosote,
and is generally nasty.—Yours truly, S.G.
An itinerant wag lately went into a Ben-
demeer district (N.S.W.) pub. for a stimulant.
Observing the local policeman absorbed in
“euchre,’’ he remarked aloud to mine host: “ I
passed old Fosbery (Inspector-General) on the,
Moonbis.” X 971 dropped his hand, decamped,
and covered the distance between the pub. and
the lock-up (200 yards) in seven seconds—the
fastest time on record. This will be interesting
to card-players, as proving that a man can be
euchred even while holding the two bovvers and
joker in his hand.

The sensational Ardlamont (Scotland)
“ murder case,” which ended in the trial of Mon-
son for the alleged shooting of Lieutenant Ham-
brough, and his acquittal, had an atmosphere of
wealth about it which has since been dissipated,
llambrough was a full-blown military officer whose
life was insured for <£20,000, and Monson was said to
be iu treaty for the purchase of the Ard lament estate
for J 580.000. Also the parties were all more or less
“ well-connected,”, and there was an aristocratic
flavour about everything which was suggestive of
the wicked peer in the London Journal. iTet
the Hambrough family, it now appears, consisted
only of the deceased and a shabby, beery old
father, who lived On a small annuity in obscure
watering-places; and Monson was a drinky ad-
venturer whose sole asset at the time of the tragedy,
was a pawn-ticket relating to an advance of 10s.
on a battered silver watch; If Hambrough shot
himself he probably did it to get away from his
only surviving parent, and if Monson shot him he
most likely did it in order to get a free ride as far
as Edinburgh on Ins way back to civilisation. If
he hadn’t been taken there by the state to be tried
lie would probably have, had to walk, and his boots
wouldn’t have held out for the journey.

A DOUBLE: WRECK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have felt sorry for Humphreys ever since, in a
moment of confidence, he told me his tale.
Of course it was a woman-trouble without a
u i • • ■ . n,
woman would be insipid as saltless soup. She
knew he was married, but yet once he was near
her, and his trembling:, passion-laden voice greeted
her ears, and those dark, sentimental eyes of his
*“•?»7•«r
into forgetfulness, and she was his, did he choose,
body and soul. Why he hadn’t married her when
he could, for they were friends of many years’
standing, is a mystery. Why he had married the
woman who was his wife is a still greater mystery.
It did not take him many months to discover that
he had made the grand mistake of his life, and
then the trouble began.
At first she used merely to nod to him as they
passed one another. Then after months there came
an evening when they stopped and spoke again.
It wasn’t his fault altogether that it happened,
every thing seemed to continue to tempt him. It
was dusk. He held her hand again in his own,
and as he gazed into her eyes he saw they were
filled frith tears. There was no one near and he
kissed her.
From this time matters progressed swiftly—
bolted, in fact-and the social air of the township
was becoming sultry. A smash was inevitable,
so everyone thought, but scandal was robbed of a
heavy meal just when it was most hungry. It
happened thus. To-day, Humphreys was worth
nothing, hoped for nothing, sought for nothing
but an equivalent return of this passion that was
eating out his very life. To-morrow ! and he was
worth over £2o,ooo—the first prize in a racing-
sweep. He accepted the stroke of good fortune
in a manner most annoying to his many acquain-
tances. He didn’t get drunk, he didn’t “ shout ”
for a single soul, and, worse still, he didn’t hand
over a single dime to the local charities, and was
accordingly anathematised by the clergy.
No, he didn’t part with a penny of it. One
idea now monopolised his mind.
He had been smoking on his verandah the
whole day. Occasionally his wife came out and
spoke to him. He never heard her, and she
turned away with a sad smile overclouding her
face. At length evening came, and as soon as the
gloaming had passed away and the blackness of
night set in, he took his hat and went out. Ter
minutes later he was with “her,” straining her
to bis heart the while his hot, fierce kisses dashed
upon her lips, neck and forehead.
“Listen!” he said, “we have suffered enough,
vou and I, we will suffer no more. If I take you
away froin here now, with me, each old hag and
every ribald tongue in the country about will be
bandying with your name, beside she would suffer
too keenly perhaps, and I’ll save her all I can.
To-morrow week I leave for England; she goes
with me. In four months-after I have provided
for her and found her a home with some of my
people—l shall be back in Sydney, and there, my
darling, my love, my life, there you must meet
me ! Then, my heart’s idol, you and I together
will sip happiness to the full in some clime far
distant from this, and dream away our lives
beneath the summer of a lasting love ! ”
She endeavoured to remonstrate with him, for
she was pure of mind, great as was this passion
that was upon her. But he—he only strained her
the closer to him, and his lips smothered the
denial to his pleading that rose to hers. Ten
minutes later they parted and she had vowed to
do all he had asked.
It was a bright, warm day in November that
found Humphreys back in Sydney again. It was
just 12 o’clock, and he was not to meet her till
four in the afternoon, so he went for a leisurely
stroll around the streets. At least, his walk was
leisurely, but his heart was galloping. Suddenly
he started and stopped. Yes! it was she ! clad,
just as she had promised to meet him, in the way
he liked best, with a spotlessly white dress, and
little round sailor hat. In a moment he was at
her side.
“ Helen! ”
She turned sharply, and when she saw him
her cheeks blanched as white as the dress she
wore, and she put her hand gently within his.
“ Forgive me, Jack, if you can, for it is all over
between us. I thought it over after you had gone,
and decided as I thought best for yon. I didn’t
fear to keep my oath to you, Jack, but I knew if
I did that it would be your ruin. I didn’t tell
you what I intended doing in my letter, for I
knew that to do so would only make it the harder
for us both. Forgive me, Jack, for I got married
a week ago.”
He looked at her for a moment, and then
repeated, with a low moan of anguish : “Married!
Great God, and I am a widower !”
At this moment a bearded man came out from a
shop close to where they were standing, and, ad-
vancing to. them, laid his hand tenderly on her
arm. “ Now, beauty, I’m at your service again !”
The words were as poison to Humphreys. He
turned and fled.
Yes, it was quite true. Humphreys’ wife had
died in the first week of her arrival in London,
and he had refrained from recording the fact in
his letters to Australia, thinking it wiser to break
the news to her with his own lips.
Is she happy? No; how can she be? To her
husband she is a dutiful wife. But that molten
stream of passion and longing still rages within
her bosom, never lessening, but consuming her day
by day.
Humphreys did not suicide. I met him a day
or two ago, and, in reply to a suggestion of mine
that he should travel, ho shook his head : “No; I
prefer to live on here, waiting and hoping.”
“ To what end ?” I asked.
“ Waiting and hoping for him to die.”
M. B.B.
“Ido so hate being thought of only as
Carlyle’s wife.”—Herself.
Cabled that Mr. James Huddart is being
publicly eulogised for his enterprise re the estab-
lishment of a new steam service between Canada
and England, Mr. Huddart is not very big, but
he is as game a man as ever stood under a hat, and
never knows what it is to be beaten. In point of
pluck he can only be compared to a tiger-cat.
There are the materials for a very beautiful
row in the address of Moderator MTnnes to
N.S.W. Presbyterian Assembly. He has denied
the verbal inspiration of the Bible, and Rev.
Dill-Macky has armed himself and has charged
the windmill of scientific truth. “Satan,” he says,
“is always striving to conquer men”; and he
implies that Old Nick has conquered the Mode-
rator.
Sydney Domain-Midnight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, hapless girl, your haggard wares
So ghastly in the gleaming light—
Your painted cheeks, your burning eyes,
Have little charms for me to-night.
My blood is hot, my brain awhirl,
I know the truth of what you tell—
I feel the shame a man must feel,
And guess your mournful fate too well.
With failing voice and racking cough,
Doomed and devoted as thou art,
Poor victim, go thy joyless way,
And of the gloom become a part.
Who next? A limping, shuffling waif—
With “ Have you got a sixpence, boss
I was not always on the cadge,
And only want to get_ a doss.
I got smashed up, not long ago,
You see my shoulder is not right.
Somehow, I think it never will
With camping out so much at night.
Thanks—Ah, you don’t knowwhat it means ;
Well, then, God send you never may.”
And down the gloom between the trees
This crippled nightbird limped away.
Who art thou? moaning in thy sleep
Stretched on a seat beneath a lamp.
Thy hands are clasped beneath thine head,
Thy silvered hair with dew is damp,
Thy frozen face, so pinched and grey,
Of hunger and privation speaks.
And then, maybe, it is not dew
But tears that sparkle on thy cheeks.
Full fifty years of storm and stress
Have been thy lot and this thy gain—
A vagrant in these workless days
Camned out in storm and wind and rain.
Who crouches near a flickering gleam
Half dozing o’er some evening rag ?
He asks the time. “ What, only one !
God ! How these midnight moments drag !”
Another yonder sneaks about,
Whose hunger will not let him sleep,
To find what serves him for a meal
He fossicks on the garbage-heap.
And listen from the gloom below
Whose heart-appalling accents tell
That helpless children in the night
Weep in this Imager-smitten hell.
I hate you with undying hate,
Queen city of the Sunny South,
Because about your parks and streets
There moaneth many a hungry mouth
That calleth on a callous Christ
And asketh all in vain for bread,
And where the lights from mansions gleam
Must seek the rocks to find a bed.
Your mourning garb may soon be worn
When mjdnight flames dispel the dark,
For men are crying, “ Bread or 8100d —
We’ll starve no longer in the Park.”
Sydney, 10th Feb., 1894,
CURLEW
Glaring notice on a country pub. : “ 2th's
beer soled here.” By the way, Tooth’s beer has
been brewed in N.S.W. for over 60 years.
Thus a hot-headed pastoralisfc who grazes
some 10,000 cattle in the Burke district (Q.), rt
stock-tax imposed by the M'Tlwraithian Govern-
ment :
My station makes £2OOO per annum, of which £5OO goes
for tucker and stores —(he balance for rent and taxes
imposed by a Queen-street Government to foster Southern
industries. Government imposes the tax but cannot
collect it, and if we band together I am prepared to con-
tribute £250, find horses for and feed 30 men to resist the
demands of the Queen-street Government.
Now, if a Labour man had said this!
The advfcs. on the backs of M.L, stamps
have served to bowl out several well-planned
swindles. The latest was that of a person who
was doing a lucrative business in manufacturing
p.n.’s, said to have been signed by a person
deceased, who left a large estate. In each case
deceased’s signature was written across the stamp,
and the cocoa-advt. on the back of the stamp gave
the scheme away. At the date of the document
there had been an infant’s food advt., or some-
thing like that, on the stamp, and the cocoa-
announcement didn’t appear till long afterwards.
This is where the forger’s troubles always come
in, for the advts. are not dated, and, infixing up a
back-dated document, it is almost impossible to
find out whether the stamp of that period had a
cheap, slop clothing notice on it or a puff re some-
body’s blucher boots.
The fine Roman hand of the Camphell-
town Herald editor, in a leading article on the
local dam :
For many years there has been at the southern end of
Campbelltown a filthy, stagnant pond which only a half-
civilized soulless community would for one hour tolerate.
Only that there is no spirit amongst the people, only that
they are a body of arrant cowards, a dozen of (hem would
walk in a body with picks and shovels and let the water
away, . . . But in poor Campbelltown the people are
always comatose. They do not even thaw or wake up
during the summer months. If they recover a little
vitality it is only with a view of having asmoke. Give
the majority of them plenty of tobacco and 1 hey are all
right. They have no ambition beyond the pipe or cigar.
If the people of the township are such miserable cravens
that they will not move, let the farmers take the master
up. . . . Surely out of so large a community they are
not all lazy cowards, and unworthy the stock from which
they sprang ! Let them ask themselves how long their
fathers or grandfathers would, in their day, put up with
such an injustice. . . . Truly the days of bhivalrv are
fast departing and in the place of men we are rearing
brainless, useless, cowardly pigmies, who simply grin while
their rights are being filched from them and who enjoy
being humbugged.
And the band played “Scots wha hae.” But the
sleepers only stirred feebly and said “Dam !”

Readers of The Bulletin, when-
ever situated, will greatly oblige hy
informing the Managing Director of
any locality where they may he unable
to procure the paper, and naming
any reliable person who would pro-
bably undertake its sale.
New South Wales Fresh Food & Ice Co.,
LIMITED.
The Y. F. & I. 00. have arrangements concluded for full Supplies of Fish during the. Lenten Season.
The Fresh Food and Ice Co. have exclusive control of the refrigerators on the Union Co.’s steamships Waihora, Mararoa
and Wairarapa, and have arranged with the Union S.S. Go. to run constantly during the Lenten season between New Zealand
Sidney. London with English Fish, is due on 23rd February.
For* Local Supplies we draw largely on the South Coast fishermen, and just now it is estimated fully 100 men are actively
engaged procuring fish for our Company. ■ •
